Rahn — Pledge
beginner
12 min
Introduction to the Book of Rahn: Definition, Evidence, Pillars, and Conditions
Prefatory discussion covering definition, proof, pillars, conditions, and benefits of rahn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the key elements of the rahn contract according to the Hanafi school as outlined by Ibn Nujaym.

Core Idea
Rahn is a gratuitous contract requiring the mortgagor's offer, the mortgagee's possession, and the pledged item being separable property.

This lesson opens the Book of Rahn (Pledge) from al-Bahr al-Raiq. The author first explains why this chapter follows hunting—both are means of acquiring wealth—then enumerates ten topics that systematically define, prove, and condition the pledge contract. We will walk through each topic to build a precise Hanafi understanding of rahn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ten topics of rahn
The passage lists ten areas: linguistic meaning, evidence, pillar, condition for bindingness, condition for validity, ruling, cause, description, legal definition, and benefits. Recognize this as an outline for the entire chapter.
2
Analyze linguistic meaning and evidence
Rahn literally means 'confinement' (habs). The Quranic verse (74:38) confirms this, and the primary evidence is the command in 2:283 to take a pledge during debt transactions.
3
Differentiate pillar and conditions
The pillar (rukn) is the mortgagor's offer (ijab). Acceptance (qabul) is a condition, not a pillar, because rahn is a gratuitous contract (tabarru'). The binding condition is possession (qabd) by the mortgagee. Validity conditions: the item must be specifically identified, separated, free from others' rights, and be property (mal).
A pledge for non-property claims (hadd, qisas, manumission) is invalid.
4
Understand the ruling and cause
The mortgagee has the right to hold the item (habs) until the debt is paid, and upon the mortgagor's death he has priority over other creditors. The sabab (cause) is the need for security in lending.
5
Note the description and benefits
The pledge is a trust (amanah) in the mortgagee's hands, per the majority of scholars. Benefits include relieving the debtor from searching and providing the creditor with security.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرهن وجه مناسبة كتاب الرهن لكتاب الصيد من حيث إن كل واحد من الرهن والصيد سبب لتحصيل المال والكلام في الرهن يقع في مواضع : الأول في معناه لغة. والثاني في دليله. والثالث في ركنه والرابع في شرط لزومه والخامس في شرط جوازه والسادس في حكمه. والسابع في سببه . والثامن في صفته والتاسع في معناه عند الفقهاء. والعاشر في محاسنه. أما معناه لغة فهو عبارة عن الحبس بأي شيء كان قال الله تعالى كل نفس بما كتسبت رهينة ﴾ [المدثر : ۳۸] أي محبوسة بما كسبت من المعاصي. يقال رهنت الشيء وارتهنته والجمع رهن ورهون ورهان والرهن المرهون تسمية بالمصدر وأما دليله فقوله تعالى فرهن مقبوضة ﴾ [البقرة : ۲۸۳] أمر يأخذ الرهن وقبضه حال المداينة وأما ركنه فهو الإيجاب وهو قول الراهن رهنت عندك هذا الشيء بمالك على من الدين أو خذه والقبول شرط له لأن الرهن عقد تبرع لأنه لم يستوجب الرهن بذاته شيئاً والتبرع يتم بالإيجاب من غير قبول حتى لو حلف لا يرهن فرهن ولم يقبل الآخر يحنث . وأما الرابع وهو شرط اللزوم وهو القبض. وأما الخامس وهو شرط الجواز فكونه مقسوماً مفرزاً فارغاً عن الشغل بحق الغير، وأن يكون الرهن بحيث يمكن الاستيفاء منه كالدين حتى لا يصح الرهن بما ليس بمال كالحدود والقصاص والعتق . وأما حكمه فملك المرتهن المرهون في حق الحبس حتى يكون أحق بإمساكه إلى وقت ايفاء الدين في حال الحياة، وأما إذا مات الراهن فهو أحق به من سائر الغرماء فيستوفي منه دينه وما فضل فهو للغرماء. وأما سببه فهو الحاجة إليه لأن الإنسان قد لا يقرضه مجاناً من غير رهن أو يصبر عليه بغير رهن. وأما صفته قال عامة العلماء بأن الرهن مضمون على المرتهن كما سيأتي بيانه. وأما التاسع وهو تفسيره شرعاً فسيتكلم عليه المؤلف . وأما العاشر وهو محاسنه فهو فك عسرة الطلب عن الراهن ووثوق لا يجد من
Book of Rahn. The reason for the suitability of the Book of Rahn following the Book of Hunting is that both rahn and hunting are means of acquiring wealth. And the discussion on rahn falls into ten topics: First, its linguistic meaning. Second, its evidence. Third, its pillar. Fourth, the condition for its bindingness. Fifth, the condition for its validity. Sixth, its ruling. Seventh, its cause. Eighth, its description. Ninth, its meaning according to jurists. Tenth, its benefits. As for its linguistic meaning, it is an expression for confinement/holding by any means. Allah said: 'Every soul is held in pledge (rahinah) for what it has earned' [Al-Muddaththir: 38], i.e., confined due to the sins it earned. It is said 'rahan-tu al-shay'a' and 'irtahantu-hu', and the plural is rihan, rihun, and rihan. 'Al-rahn' (the object pledged) is named by the infinitive. As for its evidence, it is the saying of Allah: 'So a pledge (rahn) [given] in hand' [Al-Baqarah: 283], a command to take the pledge and its possession at the time of debt. As for its pillar, it is the offer (ijab), which is the mortgagor's saying 'I have pawned this item with you for your due or your debt' or 'take it', and acceptance is a condition for it because rahn is a gratuitous contract; it does not require consideration, and a gratuitous contract is completed by offer alone without acceptance, such that if one swore not to mortgage and then mortgaged and the other did not accept, he would have broken his oath. And the fourth (condition for bindingness) is possession (qabd). And the fifth (condition for validity) is that the item must be partitioned, separated, free from the right of others, and that the pledge must be such that the debt can be recovered from it, so that a pledge for something that is not considered property, such as hadd penalties, qisas, and manumission, is not valid. And as for its ruling, the mortgagee owns the pledged item in terms of retention (habs), so that he has more right to hold it until the debt is paid during lifetime. And if the mortgagor dies, he (the mortgagee) has more right to it than other creditors, so he collects his debt from it, and what remains is for the creditors. And as for its cause, it is need for it, because a person may not lend for free without a pledge, or forbear without a pledge. And as for its description, the majority of scholars say that the pledge is a trust in the hands of the mortgagee, as will be explained. And the ninth (its legal definition) will be discussed by the author. And the tenth (its benefits) are relieving the debtor from seeking and providing security.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Book of Rahn (Pledge) in al-Bahr al-Raiq. The author first explains why the book on hunting is followed by the book on rahn: both are means of acquiring wealth. Then he enumerates ten topics that will be covered regarding rahn: its linguistic meaning, evidence, pillar, condition for bindingness, condition for validity, ruling, cause, description, legal definition, and benefits. He then elaborates on each. Linguistically, rahn means 'confinement' (habs), derived from the Quranic verse 'كل نفس بما كسبت رهينة' (every soul is held in pledge). The evidence for its legality is the Quranic command in Surah al-Baqarah (2:283) to take a pledge during debt transactions. The pillar is the offer (ijab) from the mortgagor, while acceptance is a condition but not a pillar because rahn is a gratuitous contract; it is completed by offer alone. The binding condition is possession (qabd) by the mortgagee. The validity condition is that the pledged item must be specifically identified, separated, free from others' rights, and capable of being used to satisfy the debt (i.e., it must be property). Thus, pledging for non-property claims like hadd, qisas, or manumission is invalid. The ruling (hukm) is that the mortgagee has the right to hold the item (habs) until the debt is paid; he has priority over other creditors upon the mortgagor's death. The cause (sabab) is the need for security in lending. The description (sifah) is that the pledge is a trust (amanah) in the mortgagee's hands. The legal definition is deferred. The benefits include relieving the debtor's difficulty in finding a lender and providing reassurance.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A contract whereby a property is given as security for a debt, held by the creditor as a trust until the debt is paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the pillar (rukn) of the rahn contract?
A) Possession (qabd)
B) The offer (ijab) from the mortgagor
C) Acceptance (qabul) from the mortgagee
D) Both offer and acceptance

Key Takeaway
Rahn is a gratuitous contract formed by the mortgagor's offer, requiring possession for bindingness, and the pledged item must be property, specific, and unencumbered.

Rahn — Pledge
intermediate
6 min
Rulings on Invalid Pledge and Definition of Rahn
Rulings on invalid pledges and definition of rahn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an invalid pledge when the pawned item is destroyed, and explain the definition of rahn as confinement of a thing for a right from which satisfaction can be obtained.

Core Idea
An invalid pledge does not extinguish the debt; the debt remains even if the pawned item is destroyed.

This lesson covers two aspects: first, the rulings on invalid pledges (rahn fasid) as transmitted from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and second, the legal definition of a valid pledge. We will see that an invalid pledge does not transfer the risk to the pledgee, and the debt remains intact. Then we will analyse the definition of rahn as 'confinement of a thing for a right', clarifying its scope and implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalid pledge scenarios
Read the rulings from Ibn Sama'a (from Abu Yusuf) and Nawadir Hisham (from Muhammad) about pledging half a house and delivering the whole house, and about a pledge for the price of wine. Note that the rulings state the debt is not waived by destruction.
These are nawadir (rare) transmissions but consistent with the Hanafi principle.
2
Understand the legal consequence
In an invalid pledge, the pawned item is held as a trust (amanah) in the hands of the pledgee. Therefore, if it is destroyed without his fault, the debt remains owed. The pledgee is not a guarantor.
Contrast with a valid pledge where the item is a trust as well, but the debt is secured by the item's value.
3
Analyse the definition of rahn
The author defines rahn as 'confinement of a thing for a right from which satisfaction can be obtained like a debt'. Identify the key elements: confinement (habs), a thing (shay'), a right (haqq), and the condition that the right can be satisfied from the thing.
The term 'thing' is broad; it includes both specific items and unspecified portions.
4
Examine the exclusion of specific objects
The commentator explains that pledge is primarily for debts (unspecified rights). For specific objects (ayn), pledge is invalid unless the object is itself guaranteed (e.g., a usurped item, dower). Understand the reasoning: the right must be capable of being satisfied from the thing.
This is a key distinction between rahn and kafalah (suretyship).
5
Distinguish formation from binding
An objection is raised: the definition mentions confinement, but confinement is only binding after possession. The answer is that the definition refers to the 'meaning' of confinement that exists upon contract formation, even if the binding is postponed.
This shows the importance of the difference between inqid (conclusion) and luzum (binding).

Source Text

الدين وفي الجامع الكبير : لو اشتری مسلم خمراً ورهن بثمنه رهناً فضارع الرهن عنده لا يضمن لأنه رهن باطل في الأول ينعقد فاسداً والله أعلم. وسيأتي له مزيد بيان عند قوله مضمون بأقل من قيمته». وفي الكبرى : البعض وإن كان الموجب الأصلي رد العين ورد القيمة مخلص فلا يجب الضمان إلا بعد الهلاك بالقبض السابق ولهذا تعتبر قيمته بالقبض فيكون رهناً لوجود وجود سبب وجوبه فيصح كما في الكفالة بخلاف الأعيان الأمانة ا Ibn Sama'a narrated from Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on both, that if a person pledges half a house and hands over the house to the pledgee, and it is destroyed, nothing of the debt is waived. Similarly, it is mentioned in Nawadir Hisham from Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on both, that in an invalid pledge, the debt is not waived by its destruction. In al-Jami' al-Kabir: If a Muslim buys wine and pledges a pledge for its price, then the pledge is deposited with him, he is not a guarantor because the pledge is void; in the first instance it is concluded as invalid. And Allah knows best. There will be further clarification at his statement 'guaranteed by the lesser of its value'. In al-Kubra: If he stipulates that he must guarantee the excess over the debt, the condition is void. He (the author) said: (It is the confinement of a thing for a right from which satisfaction can be obtained like a debt). This is its definition in the Sharia, thus said the commentator. He said: His statement 'like a debt' indicates that pledge is only permissible for a debt because it is a right that can be satisfied from the pledge, due to its lack of specification. As for a specific object (ayn), it cannot be satisfied from the pledge, and pledge is not permissible for it unless it is guaranteed by itself, like a usurped item, dower, compensation for khul', and blood money for intentional homicide because the original obligator is its equivalent or value, and returning the specific object is not a way out according to the majority, and hence suretyship for it is valid and release from its value is valid. And the obligation of zakah is prevented from him who is in possession of it, and his wealth is to the extent of its value. If the obligatory were the specific object, these rulings would not hold. And according to some, even if the original obligator is returning the specific object, returning the value is a way out, so guarantee is not obligatory except after destruction with prior possession, and hence its value is considered at the time of possession, so it becomes a pledge due to the existence of the reason for its obligation, so it is valid, as in suretyship, unlike trust assets. End. It is said: This definition is for the complete or binding pledge; otherwise, in the formation of the pledge, confinement is not binding; rather that is through possession. It is answered: The intended meaning is that by formation, the meaning of pledge is realized, i.e., making the thing confined for a right, except that the Lawgiver gave the contracting party the right to retract as long as the pledgee has not taken possession. So before possession, the meaning of confinement exists, but it does not become binding except through possession. What is taken in the definition mentioned in the book for the pledgee is only the confinement itself, not its binding, so this definition applies to the pledge before its completion and binding as well. It is objected... And our statement 'meaning of confinement' etc. because the contract does not necessitate the reality of confinement since that is through possession, rather it necessitates the meaning of confinement. The statement of Imam al-Zayla'i that his saying 'like a debt' indicates that pledge is only permissible for a debt because it is the right that can be satisfied from the pledge due to its lack of specification, we say: what is apparent from al-Kafi is that pledge is permissible for other than debt as well, as examples were mentioned. He said: The contract... And his saying 'thing' applies to whether it is specified or not, and whether it is for the entire debt or part of it, and whether the debt has been received or not. Qadikhan said: A man gave two garments to another man and said 'take'.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage begins by presenting rulings on invalid pledges (rahn fasid). The first ruling, from Abu Yusuf via Ibn Sama'a, is that if a person pledges half a house and delivers the entire house to the pledgee, then the house is destroyed, the debt is not reduced. This is because the pledge is invalid (since only half was pledged but full possession was given), and the property is considered a trust in the pledgee's hands, so destruction does not extinguish the debt. The same principle is confirmed in Nawadir Hisham from Muhammad: in an invalid pledge, the debt remains. In al-Jami' al-Kabir, a Muslim who buys wine (a non-property) and pledges something for its price, the pledge is void, and the pledgee is not a guarantor because wine is not a valid subject of sale, so the underlying transaction is invalid. The author then mentions that in al-Kubra, a stipulation that the pledgee guarantee the excess over the debt is void. Next, the text presents the legal definition of rahn from the matn: 'It is the confinement of a thing for a right from which satisfaction can be obtained like a debt.' The commentator explains that the phrase 'like a debt' indicates that pledge is only valid for a debt (a right that is unspecified and can be satisfied from the pledged property). For specific objects (ayn), they cannot be satisfied from the pledge because they are particular, so pledge for them is invalid unless the specific object is itself guaranteed (e.g., a usurped item, dower, etc.) because the underlying obligation is to return the value or equivalent, not the specific object. The commentator discusses the difference between pledge for debt and for specific objects, and proceeds to address an objection: that this definition applies only to a completed/binding pledge, while a pledge before possession does not involve actual confinement. The rebuttal is that the definition refers to the meaning (ma'na) of confinement, which exists upon formation of the contract, even if binding is postponed to possession. The passage ends with further analysis of the word 'thing' and other conditions, and a quotation from Qadikhan.

Key Terms
الرهن الفاسد (al-rahn al-fasid): An invalid pledge that does not fulfill conditions, such as pledging part of a property without separation, or pledging for a non-debt right.
الضمان (al-daman): Guarantee; the pledgee is not a guarantor in invalid pledge unless specified.
الحق (al-haqq): Right; the right for which the pledge is held, typically a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The debt is waived
B) The debt remains
C) The debtor must pay double
D) The pledgee becomes owner

Key Takeaway
An invalid pledge does not extinguish the debt; the debt remains, and the definition of a valid pledge is 'confinement of a thing for a right from which satisfaction can be obtained, like a debt.'

Rahn — Pledge
intermediate
8 min
Definition of Rahn and Ruling on Fasid Pledge
Defines rahn and explains the effect of invalid conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of rahn, identify conditions that render it fasid, and apply the ruling that a fasid rahn makes the property guaranteed (mazmun).

Core Idea
A pledge that includes a condition contrary to its nature becomes fasid, and the pledged property is then guaranteed by the mortgagee.

Rahn (pledge) is a contract of security where an item is detained for a right. The Hanafi school emphasizes that certain conditions, if stipulated, invalidate the contract's essential nature, turning it into a fasid (defective) pledge. Understanding this distinction is crucial for ensuring that pledges remain valid and legally effective. This lesson will unpack the definition and the key ruling on invalid conditions.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of rahn
Identify the essential elements: detention (habs) of a specific item, for a right (haqq) from which satisfaction can be obtained, such as a debt. The contract becomes binding by offer and acceptance but is completed by possession.
Note the difference between the contract (aqd) and its binding effect (luzum).
2
Understand the effect of invalid conditions
If a stipulation contradicts the nature of the contract (e.g., exempting the mortgagee from liability for loss without negligence), the condition is void and the pledge becomes fasid. The property taken under a fasid rahn is guaranteed (mazmun) by the mortgagee.
The debt is not discharged by the loss of the pledged item in a fasid rahn.
3
Recognize the scope of rights secured by rahn
Rahn is not limited to debts; it can secure other guaranteed rights such as dower (mahr), compensation for khul', blood money, and usurped property (when the obligation is the value). However, amanah (trust) items are excluded.
This nuance addresses a potential misconception that rahn is only for debts.
4
Distinguish valid from invalid rahn
A valid rahn requires: a right that can be satisfied from the pledged item, offer and acceptance, and possession for bindingness. An invalid condition (e.g., no liability) makes it fasid, not batil. The contract is not void ab initio but defective.
In a fasid rahn, the mortgagee becomes a guarantor of the item's value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرهن هو حبس شيء بحق يمكن استيفاؤه منه كالدين ولزم بإيجاب وقبول ويتم بقضه قلب المرتهن بما يحصل ماله ولو ارتهن على أنه إن ضاع بغير شيء وأجاز الرهن وبطل الشرط لأنه تغيير لعقد موضوع بحكم مشروع وتبديل المشروع لا يجوز والمقبوض بحكم الرهن الفاسد مضمون. وذكر ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف رحمهما الله تعالى لو رهن نصف دار وسلم الدار إلى المرتهن وهلكت لم يذهب من الدين شيء، وهكذا ذكر في نوادر هشام عن محمد رحمهما الله تعالى أنه في الرهن الفاسد لا يذهب بهلاكه الدين وفي الجامع الكبير : لو اشتری مسلم خمراً ورهن بثمنه رهناً فضارع الرهن عنده لا يضمن لأنه رهن باطل في الأول ينعقد فاسداً والله أعلم. وسيأتي له مزيد بيان عند قوله مضمون بأقل من قيمته». وفي الكبرى : لو شرط عليه أن يضمن الفضل عن الدين فالشرط باطل . قال رحمه الله : (هو حبس شيء بحق يمكن استيفاؤه منه كالدين) وهذا حده في الشرع كذا قال الشارح وقال : قوله «كالدين» إشارة إلى أن الرهن لا يجوز إلا بالدين لأنه هو حق أمكن استيفاؤه ومن الدين لعدم تعيينه، وأما العين فلا يمكن استيفاؤها من الرهن ولا يجوز الرهن بها إلا إذا كانت مضمونة بنفسها كالمغصوب والمهر ويدل الخلع وبدل الصلح عن دم العمد لأن الموجب الأصلي فيها المثل أو القيمة ورد العين لا مخلص على ما عليه الجمهور ولهذا تصح الكفالة به والإبراء عن قيمته. ويمتنع وجوب الذكاة عمن هو في يده وماله بقدر القيمة. ولو كان الواجب هو العين لما ثبتت هذه الأحكام وعند البعض وإن كان الموجب الأصلي رد العين ورد القيمة مخلص فلا يجب الضمان إلا بعد الهلاك بالقبض السابق ولهذا تعتبر قيمته بالقبض فيكون رهناً لوجود وجود سبب وجوبه فيصح كما في الكفالة بخلاف الأعيان الأمانة ا هـ . قيل : هذا التعريف للرهن التام أو اللازم وإلا ففي انعقاد الرهن لا يلزم الحبس بل ذلك بالقبض . أجيب بأن المراد أنه يتحقق بانعقاد معنى الرهن معنى جعل الشيء محبوساً بحق إلا أن الشارع جعل للعاقد الرجوع عنه ما لم يقبض المرتهن الرهن فقبل القبض يوجد معنى الحبس ولكن لا يلزم ذلك إلا بالقبض والمأخوذ في التعريف المذكور في الكتاب للمرتهن إنما هو نفس الحبس لا لزومه فيصدق هذا التعريف على الرهن قبل تمامه ولزومه أيضاً. لو هو . يرد على معنى حبس العين بحق يمكن استيفاؤه منه لكان أولى. وقولنا على معنى حبس إلى آخره لأن العقد لا يوجب حقيقة الحبس لأنها بالقبض بل يوجب نفس الحبس، وقول الإمام الزيلعي أن قوله كالدين إشارة إلى أن الرهن لا يجوز إلا بالدين لأنه هو الحق الممكن استيفاؤه من الرهن لعدم تعيينه قلنا المتبادر إليه من الكافي أنه يجوز الرهن بغير الدين أيضاً كما ذكرت أمثاله .
Book of Pledge: It is the detention of a thing due to a right from which satisfaction can be obtained, like a debt, and it becomes binding by offer and acceptance and is completed by possession. The mortgagee's flipping? to obtain his property. If one pledges on condition that if it is lost without anything, the pledge is valid and the condition is void because it is a change to a contract established by a lawful ruling, and changing what is lawful is not permissible. What is taken under an invalid pledge is guaranteed. Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Abu Yusuf: if someone pledges half a house and hands over the house to the mortgagee and it perishes, nothing is lost from the debt. Similarly, it is mentioned in Nawadir Hisham from Muhammad that in an invalid pledge, the debt does not lapse by the destruction [of the pledge]. In al-Jami' al-Kabir: if a Muslim buys wine and pledges a pawn for its price, the pawn is not guaranteed because it is a void pledge in the first instance, it is concluded invalid. And Allah knows best. There will be further explanation when he says 'guaranteed for the lesser of its value'. In al-Kubra: if he stipulates that he will guarantee the surplus over the debt, the condition is void. He (the author) said: (It is the detention of a thing due to a right from which satisfaction can be obtained, like a debt) This is its definition in the Shariah. The commentator said: his statement 'like a debt' indicates that a pledge is only valid for a debt, because it is a right from which satisfaction can be obtained from the pledge, due to its lack of specificity. As for a specific item (ayn), satisfaction cannot be obtained from the pledge, and a pledge is not valid for it unless it is guaranteed in itself, like a usurped object, dower, the compensation for khul', and the blood money for deliberate homicide, because the primary obligation in these is the equivalent or value, and returning the specific item is not a discharge according to the majority. Hence, suretyship (kafalah) for it is valid, and release from its value is valid. The obligation of slaughter (zakah) is not obligatory on the one in whose hand it is, and his wealth is equal to its value. If the obligation were the specific item, these rulings would not apply. According to some, even if the primary obligation is the return of the specific item, returning the value is a discharge, so guarantee is not obligated except after destruction, due to the prior possession. Hence its value at the time of possession is considered, so it becomes a pledge due to the existence of the cause of its obligation, so it is valid, as in kafalah, contrary to trust items. It was said: This definition is for a complete or binding pledge. Otherwise, at the contract formation, detention is not binding; rather that is by possession. The response is that it means that by contract the meaning of pledge is realized, which is making the thing detained due to a right, except that the Lawgiver allowed the contracting party to revoke as long as the mortgagee has not taken possession. So before possession, the meaning of detention exists, but it is not binding except by possession. What is taken in the definition mentioned in the book is the detention itself, not its bindingness, so this definition applies to the pledge before its completion and bindingness as well. It is objected that it would be better to say 'the meaning of detaining the item due to a right from which satisfaction can be obtained'. Our saying 'the meaning of detention' etc., because the contract does not bring into existence the reality of detention, since that is by possession, but rather it brings into existence the meaning of detention. The statement of Imam al-Zayla'i that his saying 'like a debt' indicates that a pledge is only valid for a debt, because it is the right from which satisfaction can be obtained from the pledge due to its lack of specification, we say: the apparent understanding from al-Kafi is that a pledge is also valid for something other than a debt, as its examples were mentioned.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq with its sharh. It opens by defining rahn (pledge) as the detention of an item for a right from which satisfaction can be obtained, such as a debt. The pledge becomes binding by offer and acceptance and is completed by possession. Immediately, the sharh addresses a conditional stipulation that the pledge is not guaranteed if lost without negligence; such a condition is void because it changes the nature of the contract, making it fasid. In a fasid rahn, the property taken is guaranteed (mamnoon) by the mortgagee. The text then quotes Ibn Sama'ah from Abu Yusuf and Nawadir Hisham from Muhammad confirming that the debt is not discharged by the loss of a fasid pledge. It also cites al-Jami' al-Kabir on a Muslim buying wine and pledging for its price; that pledge is void (batil) because the underlying transaction is prohibited. The sharh then explains the definition: the phrase 'like a debt' indicates that rahn is primarily for debts, but it also applies to other guaranteed rights such as dower, usurped property, khul' compensation, and blood money, because in these cases the primary obligation is the value or equivalent, not the specific item. The sharh further discusses that the definition in the matn refers to the meaning of detention (habs) itself, not its binding effect. Before possession, the contract creates the meaning of detention but it is not binding; the pledgor can revoke. This is the Hanafi position that rahn is a gratuitous contract (tabarru'). The passage ends by responding to Imam al-Zayla'i's view that rahn is only for debts, citing al-Kafi which permits rahn for other rights.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): Detention of an item as security for a right from which satisfaction can be obtained.
الرهن الفاسد (al-rahn al-fasid): A pledge contract that is defective due to an invalid condition, such as exempting the mortgagee from liability for loss without negligence.
القبض (al-qabd): Physical possession of the pledged item by the mortgagee, which is necessary for the contract to become binding (lazim).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pledgor stipulates that the mortgagee is not liable if the pledge is lost without negligence, what is the ruling?
A) The condition is valid and the pledge is binding.
B) The condition is void and the pledge becomes fasid, making the property guaranteed.
C) The pledge is void and the mortgagee must return the property.
D) The pledge is valid and the condition is upheld because it is a voluntary contract.

Key Takeaway
The essence of rahn is detention for a right; invalid conditions that alter this nature render the pledge fasid and the property guaranteed.

Cases of Lost Pledge and Khiyar al-Shart
Presents case studies on loss of non-specified pledges and ruling on khiyar al-shart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on loss of a non-specified pledge and explain the permissibility of stipulating an option (khiyar al-shart) for the pledgor.

Core Idea
In a pledge, taking more than authorized makes the creditor liable; khiyar al-shart is valid only for the pledgor.

This lesson presents several case studies from Qadikhan and Abu Yusuf that illustrate the consequences when a pledge is lost under ambiguous circumstances. It also clarifies the specific ruling on stipulating an option (khiyar al-shart) in a pledge contract. By examining these cases, you will understand how the Hanafi school applies principles of authorization and analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and authorization
Determine what the debtor authorized the creditor to take (e.g., one of two garments, one of two pledges, etc.) and what the creditor actually took.
Authorized vs. unauthorized taking is key.
2
Analyze the legal analogy
For each case, note the analogy used by Imam Muhammad (e.g., giving a hundred dirhams to take twenty). This helps derive the ruling.
The analogy clarifies the principle of excessive taking.
3
State the ruling on debt and liability
For each scenario, state whether the debt is discharged or remains, and who bears the loss.
Compare the first and second cases: one results in no loss to debt, the other in proportional loss.
4
Extract the general principle
Formulate the underlying rule: unauthorized taking of a pledge does not affect the debt; the creditor is liable for excess loss.
This principle applies analogously to other similar cases.
5
Apply the ruling on khiyar al-shart
For the option part, distinguish between the pledgor and the mortgagee: why the option is invalid for the mortgagee but valid for the pledgor.
The mortgagee already has the right to cancel before possession, so no need for stipulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال قاضيخان رجل دفع إلى رجل ثوبين وقال خذ أيهما شئت بالمائة التي علي فأخذهما فضاعا في يده قال الثالث : لا يذهب من الدين شيء وجعله بمنزلة رجل عليه عشرون درهماً يدفع المديون إلى الطالب مائة وقال خذ منها عشرين بدينك فضاعت المائة قبل أن يأخذ منها عشرين ضاعت من مال المديون والدين على حاله، ولو قال خذ أحدهما رهناً بدينك فأخذهما فضاعا في يده وقيمتهما سواء قال الثالث: يذهب نصف قيمة كل واحد منهما بالدين إن كان مثل الدين: رجل عليه مائة فأعطى الدائن ثوباً وقال خذ هذا ببعض حقك فقبضه وهلك يهلك بقيمته قال أبو يوسف : لما شاء المرتهن أخذ الرهن ولم يدفع شيئاً فضاع في يده. قال أبو يوسف : عليه قيمة الرهن أقرض آخر خمسين درهماً فقال المقرض لا يكفيك هذا القدر ولكن أبعث لك ما يكفيك فبعث فدفع إليه فضاع في يده فعلى المرتهن الأقل من قيمة الرهن ومن الخمسين. واشتراط خيار الشرط ثلاث أيام في الرهن غير جائز في المرتهن لأنه يملك فسخه من غير خيار الشرط فلا فائدة في اشتراطه، وللراهن جائز لأنه يحتاج إلى الخيار فيه وهو في معنى البيع فيصح إثبات الخيار له فيه؛ كذا في الأصل .
Qadikhan said: A man gave another two garments and said: 'Take whichever you wish for the hundred that I owe you.' He took both, and they were lost in his hand. According to the third [scholar], nothing of the debt is lost. He considered it like the case of a man who owes twenty dirhams and the debtor gives the creditor one hundred dirhams and says: 'Take twenty of it for your debt.' Then the hundred is lost before he takes twenty; it is lost from the debtor's wealth and the debt remains as it was. And if he said: 'Take one of them as a pledge for your debt,' and he took both, and they were lost in his hand, and their value was equal, according to the third, half the value of each goes towards the debt if it is like the debt. A man owed one hundred and gave the creditor a garment and said: 'Take this for some of your right.' He took possession and it perished. It perishes by its value. Abu Yusuf said: Whenever the mortgagee wishes, he takes the pledge and does not pay anything. They were lost in his hand. Abu Yusuf said: Upon him is the value of the pledge. Another person lent fifty dirhams. The lender said: 'This amount is not enough for you, but I will send you what suffices.' He sent and gave it to him, and it was lost in his hand. Upon the mortgagee is the lesser of the value of the pledge and the fifty. The stipulation of an option (khiyar al-shart) for three days in a pledge is not permissible for the mortgagee because he can cancel it without an option condition, so there is no benefit in stipulating it. For the pledgor it is permissible because he needs an option in it, and it is analogous to sale, so establishing an option for him is valid. This is in al-Asl.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage contains several case studies from Qadikhan and rulings from Abu Yusuf. The first case: a debtor gives two garments to his creditor and says 'take whichever you wish' but the creditor takes both and they are lost. According to the third (probably Imam Muhammad), the debt is not discharged because the creditor took more than authorised; the loss is from the debtor's property. This is analogised to a debtor giving the creditor a hundred dirhams and telling him to take twenty; if the hundred is lost before the twenty is taken, it is from the debtor's wealth. The second case: the debtor says 'take one as a pledge' but the creditor takes both; if both have equal value, half the value of each goes towards the debt, i.e., the creditor is deemed to have taken one of them as a pledge and the other without right. The third case involves a partial payment: a debtor gives a garment as a pledge for part of the debt; if it perishes, it perishes at its value. Abu Yusuf rules that the mortgagee may take the pledge without paying anything, but if it is lost in his hand, he is liable for its value. A further case: a borrower sends more money than needed; if it is lost in the borrower's hand, the borrower is liable for the lesser of the value (of what he sent) and the debt? The text says 'upon the mortgagee is the lesser of the value of the pledge and the fifty.' The final part deals with khiyar al-shart: stipulating an option for three days in a pledge is invalid for the mortgagee because he can already cancel (since the contract is revocable before possession); however, it is valid for the pledgor because he needs the option to back out, and this is analogous to sale. The ruling is from al-Asl (the core of Imam Muhammad).

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The right to cancel a contract within a stipulated period (e.g., three days).
الراهن (al-raahin): The pledgor, the debtor who gives a pledge.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The mortgagee, the creditor who receives the pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a pledge contract, who may validly stipulate an option (khiyar al-shart)?
A) Both the pledgor and mortgagee.
B) Only the mortgagee.
C) Only the pledgor.
D) Neither party.

Key Takeaway
The loss of an unauthorized pledge does not discharge the debt; the option (khiyar) in a pledge is only valid for the pledgor.

Rahn — Pledge
intermediate

Pillars, Conditions, and Revocability of Pledge (Rahn)
Critical refinement of matn on pledge pillars and bindingness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pillars of rahn, explain why possession is required for bindingness, and apply the conditions of the pledged object and the rule of takhliyah.

Core Idea
Pledge is a gratuitous contract: offer and acceptance conclude it, but possession makes it binding.

This passage is a critical refinement from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq correcting a common misunderstanding about when a pledge becomes binding. The matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq appears to say the pledge becomes binding by offer and acceptance, but the commentator clarifies that it only becomes binding after possession. We will walk through the pillars, the conditions of the pledged property, the rule of takhliyah (vacating) as sufficient delivery, and the right of the pledgor to revoke before possession. This distinction has practical consequences for both parties in a modern pledge transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is rahn (pledge). The ruling: It is concluded (mun'aqid) by offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) but becomes binding (lazim) only after possession (qabd). Before possession, it is ja'iz (permissible, not binding) and the pledgor may revoke.
This is the corrected Hanafi position; the matn's wording is a slip.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof that rahn is a gratuitous contract (tabarru') and thus not binding before delivery. The commentator rejects the analogy to sale because sale is exchange (mu'awadah). He also cites the consensus of the school that takhliyah suffices for delivery, drawing an analogy to sale and distinguishing from usurpation (ghasb).
Dalil: nature of tabarru' and rejection of qiyas to sale.
3
Analyze the conditions of the object
The matn states the pledged item must be 'mahuzan, mufarraghan, mumayyazan' – isolated (not a share in undivided property), emptied (not occupied by another's right), and separated (not attached to other property). These are conditions for valid possession.
These three conditions ensure the mortgagee can take exclusive possession.
4
Understand takhliyah as delivery
Takhliyah (vacating) is the pledgor removing obstacles to possession. In Hanafi law, this suffices as delivery (taslim) for both pledge and sale. The pledgor is only responsible for what he can do; actual receipt is the act of the mortgagee. The stronger view (ad'ha) accepts takhliyah even for movables, though some require physical transfer.
Analogy to ghasb is rejected; the sarf exception is noted.
5
Apply the revocability rule
Before possession, the pledgor may revoke the pledge. This flows from the nature of tabarru': a volunteer is not bound until full delivery. Khawahirzadah clarifies: the pledge becomes binding on the pledgor by possession, and binding on the mortgagee by payment of the loan.
Malik disagrees; this is a thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولزم بإيجاب وقبول ويتم بقضه محوزاً مفرغاً مميزاً) وهذا سهو فإن الرهن لا يلزم بالإيجاب والقبول لأنه تبرع ولكنه ينعقد بهما ويتم بالقبض فيلزم به. قال في العناية : ركن الرهن الإيجاب وهو قول الراهن ،رهنت والقبول وهو قول المرتهن قبلت، ثم علل بأنه عقد والعقد ينعقد بهما، لو أورد عليه بأن صاحب المحيط صرح بأنه عقد تبرع يتم بالإيجاب فقط هو قول غالب المشايخ. وقال الإمام مالك رضي الله تعالى عنه : يلزم بالإيجاب والقبول كالبيع والإجارة وقوله «محوزاً مفرغاً مميزاً احترز بالأول عن المشاع، وبالثاني عن المشغول، وبالثالث عن المتصل إذا قبضه كذلك، ثم هذا بيان الرهن بالقول وسنبين ما رهناً بالفعل. قال رحمه الله : (والتخلية فيه وفي البيع قبض قال الشارح: والصواب أن التخلية تسليم لأنه عبارة عن رفع الموانع عن القبض وهو المسلم دون المتسلم والقبض فعل المتسلم لأنه اكتفى بالتخلية لأنه غاية ما يقدر عليه والقبض فعل لغيره فلا يكلف به وهو ظاهر الرواية، وعن الثاني أن في المنقول لا بد من النقل والأول أضح والقياس على الغصب باطل لأن قبض الرهن مشروع فيشبه البيع فاكتفى بالتخلية والغصب ليس بمشروع فلا حاجة إلى ثبوت بدون قبض حقيقة وهو النقل ووضع اليد ولا يرد النقض بالصرف لأنه لا بد فيه من القبض حقيقة لأنه ورد على خلاف القياس. قال رحمه الله: (وله أن يرجع عن الرهن ما لم يقبضه المرتهن لما ذكرنا أنه متبرع ولا لزوم على المتبرع ما لم يسلم بالكلية وفيه خلاف مالك . واختلفوا في القبض قال الشيخ الإمام المعروف بخواهر زاده : الرهن قبل القبض جائز غير لازم وإنما يصير لازماً في حق الرهن بالقبض ا هـ. وإنما يصير لازماً في حق المرتهن بالدفع وقبض الراهن الدارهم فلو قال ولهما أن يرجعا ما لم يتقابضا يصير
The author said: 'It becomes binding by offer and acceptance and is completed by possession, it being taken into possession, emptied, and separated.' This is a slip because the pledge does not become binding by offer and acceptance; it is a gratuitous contract. Rather, it is concluded by them and completed by possession, and thus becomes binding. In al-Inayah: The pillar of the pledge is the offer (the pledgor saying 'I pledge') and acceptance (the mortgagee saying 'I accept'). Then he justified it as being a contract, and a contract is concluded by them. If it is objected that the author of al-Muhit stated that it is a gratuitous contract that is completed by offer alone, that is the statement of most scholars. Imam Malik (may Allah be pleased with him) said: It becomes binding by offer and acceptance like sale and lease. His statement 'it being taken into possession, emptied, and separated' safeguards first against undivided shares, second against occupied items, third against attached items if he takes possession accordingly. Then this is the explanation of the pledge by verbal contract, and we will explain the pledge by action. The author said: 'Vacating (takhliyah) therein and in sale is possession.' The commentator said: The correct view is that takhliyah is delivery (taslim) because it is removing obstacles to possession. It is the act of the one who delivers, not the recipient. Possession is the act of the recipient. He is excused by takhliyah because it is the most he can do, and possession is the act of another, so he is not charged with it. This is the apparent of the narration. According to the second, in movables, transfer is necessary. The first is clearer. Analogy to usurpation (ghasb) is invalid because possession of a pledge is legitimate, so it resembles sale, thus takhliyah suffices. Usurpation is not legitimate, so establishment without actual possession (transfer and hand placement) is not needed. The issue of currency exchange (sarf) is not a refutation because actual possession is required there because it came contrary to analogy. The author said: 'He may revoke the pledge as long as the mortgagee has not taken possession' because we mentioned that he is a volunteer, and there is no binding on a volunteer until he fully delivers. There is a difference of opinion with Malik. They differed regarding possession. The Shaykh al-Imam known as Khawahirzadah said: The pledge before possession is permissible and not binding, and it becomes binding in relation to the pledgor by possession. And it becomes binding in relation to the mortgagee by payment and the pledgor's receipt of the dirhams. So if he said, 'Both may revoke as long as they have not exchanged,' it becomes...
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This lesson is a critical refinement (hāshiya) of the matn statement from Kanz al-Daqā’iq. The matn says that the pledge 'becomes binding by offer and acceptance and is completed by possession with the conditions of being isolated, emptied, and separated.' The commentator (probably from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq) corrects this: the pledge does NOT become binding (lāzim) by offer and acceptance alone because it is a gratuitous contract (tabarru’). Rather, it is concluded (mun’aqid) by them and becomes binding only after possession (qabḍ). This is the standard Hanafi position. He cites al-‘Ināyah stating that the rukn (pillar) is the offer and acceptance, but bindingness requires possession. The objection from Sāḥib al-Muḥīṭ that it is completed by offer alone is noted as a view of many scholars. Imām Mālik holds it binding by offer and acceptance like sale. Then the conditions of the object are explained: 'mahūzan' means it must be isolated (not a share in common property), 'mufarraghan' means it must be empty (not occupied by another’s right), and 'mumayyazan' means it must be separate (not attached to other property). Next, the rule of takhliyah (vacating) is discussed: according to the apparent of the riwāyah (ṣāḥib al-riwāyah), takhliyah counts as delivery (taslīm) in pledge and sale, because it is all the seller/pledgor can do; the actual taking (qabḍ) is the act of the mortgagee. For movables, some require physical transfer but the first view is stronger. The analogy to ghaṣb (usurpation) is rejected because ghaṣb is unlawful, so its qabḍ is different. The case of ṣarf (currency exchange) is distinguished because it requires actual qabḍ in the contract itself. Finally, the right of revocability: the pledgor may revoke before possession because he is a volunteer (mutabarri’). This is contrary to Mālik’s view. Khawāhirzādah clarifies that before possession the pledge is jā’iz (permissible) but not lāzim (binding); it becomes binding on the pledgor by possession and on the mortgagee by the payment of the loan (or by his receipt of the debt).

Key Terms
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The offer made by the pledgor, e.g., 'I pledge this to you.'
القبول (al-qabul): The acceptance by the mortgagee, e.g., 'I accept.'
التخلية (al-takhliyah): Vacating the property by removing obstacles to possession, which in Hanafi suffices as delivery (taslim) for pledge and sale.
القبض (al-qabd): The act of taking possession. In pledge, it is the act of the mortgagee. The pledgor performs takhliyah; the mortgagee performs qabd.
التسليم (al-taslim): Delivery by the seller/pledgor to the buyer/mortgagee. In Hanafi, takhliyah qualifies as taslim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of takhliyah (vacating) by the pledgor?
A) It concludes the contract of pledge.
B) It counts as delivery (taslim) and the mortgagee's possession is deemed to have occurred.
C) It is irrelevant for pledge, only for sale.
D) It makes the pledge binding even without the mortgagee's acceptance.

Key Takeaway
A pledge is concluded by offer and acceptance but becomes binding only after possession, and takhliyah (vacating) suffices as delivery for immovables and, in the preferred view, for movables as well.

Pledge: Non-Binding Before Delivery, Takhliyah as Valid Delivery
Explaining that a pledge is not binding without delivery and takhliyah suffices as delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a pledge is non-binding before delivery, explain why takhliyah suffices as delivery, and identify the conditions for the pledge to become binding.

Core Idea
A pledge is a donation – binding only upon delivery; takhliyah makes delivery complete.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, we explore a foundational rule of Hanafi pledge law: the contract is not binding until the pledgee takes possession (qabd). The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies that takhliyah – the act of clearing the property and making it fully accessible – is legally considered delivery in both pledge and sale. This lesson unpacks the nature of the contract, the significance of takhliyah, and the right of the pledgor to revoke before delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal nature of the pledge contract before delivery
Recognise that the Hanafi school classifies pledge (rahn) as a non-binding gift (tabarru') before delivery. Offer and acceptance alone create a valid but revocable contract.
This is key: the contract is valid (sahih) but not binding (ghayr lazim).
2
Understand how delivery is effected: takhliyah
Takhliyah means vacating the property and removing all obstacles to the pledgee's access. The Hanafi school considers this sufficient for delivery (qabd) in both pledge and sale, as it is the utmost the pledgor can do.
Contrast with the view requiring physical transfer (naql) in movables.
3
Note the pledgor's right to revoke before delivery
Because the contract is a gift, the pledgor may unilaterally revoke it as long as the pledgee has not taken possession. After delivery, the pledge becomes binding on both parties.
This revocability is a direct consequence of the contractual nature.
4
Recognise the correction: offer and acceptance do not make the pledge binding
The author corrects a misunderstanding: the pledge does not become binding (lazim) by offer and acceptance alone. It is contracted (mun'aqid) by them, but completed (mutamm) and binding only upon delivery.
This distinction between 'contract' (in'iqad) and 'binding' (luzum) is crucial.
5
Note the ikhtilaf with Imam Malik and intra-Hanafi difference
Imam Malik holds that the pledge becomes binding by offer and acceptance like sale and hire. Within the Hanafi school, there is a view that in movables actual transfer is required, but the relied-upon view (zahir al-riwayah) is that takhliyah suffices.
The author prefers the first view as clearer (adah).

The commentator said: the correct view is that takhliyah is delivery because it means removing obstacles to possession; it is the act of the giver, not the receiver. Possession is the act of the receiver because he suffices with takhliyah as it is the utmost he can do, and possession is the act of another, so he is not burdened with it. This is the apparent narration. From the second, in movables, transfer is necessary. The first is clearer. Analogy to usurpation is invalid because the possession of a pledge is legislated and resembles sale, so takhliyah suffices. Usurpation is not legislated, so there is no need for it to be established without actual possession, which is transfer and manual seizure. The objection of currency exchange is not valid because actual possession is necessary in it as it came contrary to analogy. He said: he may revoke the pledge as long as the pledgee has not taken possession, because we mentioned that he is a donor, and there is no binding on a donor until he gives completely. There is disagreement with Malik. They differed about possession. The Shaykh Imam known as Khawahirzadeh said: the pledge before possession is permissible and non-binding; it only becomes binding in the right of the pledgor by possession. It becomes binding in the right of the pledgee by payment and the pledgor's receipt of the dirhams. So if he said: both may revoke as long as they have not exchanged, it becomes...

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the nature of a pledge contract in Hanafi fiqh. The author explains that a pledge is not a binding contract until actual delivery (qabd) occurs. The matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) states that takhliyah—vacant possession or removing obstacles to the pledgee's access—suffices as delivery in both pledge and sale. The commentary clarifies that takhliyah is the act of the pledgor (muslim), while qabd is the act of the pledgee (mutasallim). The pledgor is not required to physically hand over the item; it is enough that he makes it available and removes any barriers. This is the relied-upon view (zahir al-riwayah). The passage also addresses the pledgor's right to revoke the contract before delivery, as it is a non-binding donation (tabarru'). After delivery, the pledge becomes binding on both parties. Various case examples illustrate the application, including scenarios where the pledge is lost before delivery, and the ruling on stipulating a condition of option (khiyar al-shart) in the pledge contract. The author corrects a common misunderstanding: the pledge is not made binding by offer and acceptance alone; it is only completed upon delivery.

Key Terms
تخلية (takhliyah): Vacant possession; the act of removing obstacles to the pledgee's access to the pledged item.
قبض (qabd): Delivery; taking possession. In pledge, it is the act of the pledgee (mutasallim) that makes the contract binding.
رهن (rahn): A contract whereby property is given as security for a debt; if the debt is not paid, the pledge may be sold to satisfy it.
خيار الشرط (khiyār al-sharṭ): A stipulated option to rescind a contract within a specified period (e.g., three days).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Upon writing the contract
D) Upon payment of the debt

Key Takeaway
A pledge is not binding until the pledgee takes possession; takhliyah suffices as valid delivery, and the pledgor may revoke before delivery.

Liability of the Pledge: It is Guaranteed by the Lesser of its Value or the Debt
Ruling with proof: the pledge is a guaranty, not a trust, with scenarios and inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of a pledge (rahn) when it perishes, apply it to three scenarios (value equal, less, or more than debt), and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i position with the supporting dalil.

Core Idea
A pledge is a guaranty: if it perishes, the debt is discharged up to the lesser of its value or the debt.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we encounter a fundamental Hanafi principle that sets the pledge apart from a mere trust. The author explains that the pledged item is 'madmūn' — a guaranty — and its loss proportionally extinguishes the debt. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its three scenarios, and the proof from the hadith and consensus, as well as the contrasting view of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the opening sentence: the pledge is guaranteed by the lesser of its value or the debt. Grasp that this means the pledge's liability is capped at the lower of the two amounts.
The phrase 'بأقل من قيمته ومن الدين' is the key.
2
Work through the three scenarios
The passage details three possibilities when the pledge perishes: (1) value equals debt → debt fully discharged; (2) value exceeds debt → debt discharged up to debt amount, surplus is trust; (3) value is less than debt → debt reduced by value, creditor pursues remainder.
Apply each to understand the proportional liability.
3
Identify the surplus as a trust
The surplus value beyond the debt is 'amānah' (trust). If lost, the pledgee is not liable for it, unless negligence occurs.
Distinguishes pledge from a full guaranty.
4
Note the opposing Shafi'i view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds the entire pledge is a trust, so no part of the debt is discharged by its loss.
This is the main inter-madhhab difference.
5
Examine the Hanafi proof
The hadith 'إذا هلك الرهن هلك الدين' (If the pledge perishes, the debt perishes) and consensus of the Companions form the basis. Consider how the hadith's wording directly links destruction of pledge to destruction of debt.
Proof is ahad hadith, zanni in transmission and meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرتهن وهو مضمون بأقل من قيمته ومن الدين فلو هلك وقيمته مثل الدين صار مستوفياً دينه وإن كان أكثر من دينه فالفضل أمانة وبقدر الدين صار مستوفياً دينه وإن كان أقل صار مستوفياً بقدر دينه ويرجع المرتهن وبالفضل) وقال الإمام الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه : الرهن كله أمانة فلا يسقط شيء من الدين .بهلاكه ولنا قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام للمرتهن الذي هلك عنده الفرس ذهب حقه وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام إذا هلك الرهن هلك الدين وأما معناه وأجمع الصحابة والتابعون على ذلك وبيان الدليلين من الجانبين في المطولات. وفي الكافي بيانه إذا رهن ثوباً قيمته عشرة بعشرة فهلك عند المرتهن يسقط دينه، وإن كان قيمة الثوب خمسة يرجع المرتهن على الراهن بخمسة أخرى، وإن كانت قيمته خمسة عشر فالفضل أمانة عندنا. وفي الينابيع : الرهن مضمون بالأقل من قيمته ومن الدين، وفائدة هذا تظهر في مسائل منها : إذا رهن عبداً بألف درهم وقيمته ألفان فابق فرده رجل من :
...the pledgee, and it (the pledge) is guaranteed by the lesser of its value or the debt. If it perishes and its value equals the debt, he is considered to have collected his debt. If it is more than his debt, the surplus is a trust, and to the extent of the debt he is considered to have collected his debt. If it is less, he is considered to have collected to the extent of his debt, and the pledgee will seek the surplus. Imam al-Shafi'i said: the entire pledge is a trust, so nothing of the debt is discharged by its loss. We have the saying of the Prophet for the pledgee whose horse perished: his right has gone, and his saying: if the pledge perishes, the debt perishes. And the Companions and Followers are unanimous on that. The explanation of both proofs from both sides is in the extended works. In al-Kafi: if he pledges a garment worth ten for ten, and it perishes with the pledgee, his debt is discharged. If the value of the garment is five, the pledgee may claim another five from the pledgor. If its value is fifteen, the surplus is a trust with us. In al-Yanabi': the pledge is guaranteed by the lesser of its value or the debt. The benefit of this appears in issues including: if he pledges a slave for a thousand dirhams and his value is two thousand, then it ran away, and a man returned him...
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage establishes the pivotal Hanafī ruling on the liability of a pledged item (rahn). Unlike a deposit (wadi'ah), which is a trust, a pledge is a guaranty (madmūn). If the pledged item is lost or perishes, the creditor's debt is reduced proportionally to the item's value, but only up to the lesser of the item's value or the debt amount. For example, if a garment worth ten is pledged for a debt of ten and it perishes, the debt is fully discharged. If the garment is worth five, the debt is reduced by five and the creditor may claim the remaining five from the debtor. If the item is worth fifteen, ten of the debt is discharged and the surplus of five is a trust (amānah) in the creditor's hands—if lost, he does not guarantee it. This ruling is supported by a hadith: 'If the pledge perishes, the debt perishes,' and by consensus of the Companions. The Shafi'ī school holds the pledge to be entirely a trust, meaning no part of the debt is discharged by its loss. The Hanafī position is thus opposed, relying on stronger textual evidence.

Key Terms
مضمون (madmūn): Guaranteed; the pledge is liable for the debt up to its value.
أمانة (amānah): A trust; an item held without liability for loss unless by negligence.
فضل (faḍl): Surplus; the excess value of the pledged item over the debt amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person pledges an item worth 100 dirhams for a debt of 80 dirhams, and the item is lost while in the pledgee's possession, what is the outcome according to the Hanafī school?
A) The debt of 80 is fully discharged, and the extra 20 is a trust.
B) The debt is reduced by 100, and the debtor owes 20.
C) The debt remains unchanged; the pledge is a trust.
D) The pledgee must pay the debtor 20 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
The pledge is a guaranty (madmūn) for the debt; its loss discharges the debt up to the lesser of its value or the debt, with the surplus being a trust.

Expenses for the Pawned Item: Dividing Liability
Ruling on expenses for pawned items with division between guarantee and trust.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how expenses (ujr, medical) for a pawned item are apportioned between pledger and pledgee based on the division of guarantee and trust.

Core Idea
The pawned item is part guaranteed by the debt and part trust; expenses are split proportionally.

In this passage from al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq, the author discusses how expenses incurred for a pawned item, such as wage for returning a runaway slave or medical treatment, are to be divided. The principle is based on the nature of the pawn: the part of the item corresponding to the debt is guaranteed, and the excess is a trust. Expenses are allocated accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expense type
Recognize whether the expense is a journey wage for returning a runaway slave or medical treatment for the pawned slave.
2
Determine the liability split
Since the slave is half guaranteed (by the debt) and half trust, the wage is divided equally between pledger and pledgee.
If the journey is three days, the wage is half on each.
3
Apply proportional division for medical treatment
Medical costs are also divided proportionally: the share corresponding to the guaranteed part is on the pledgee, and the share corresponding to the trust part is on the pledger.
4
Note the refinement
The author suggests stating that the pawn is guaranteed by the lesser of the value of the guaranteed portion and the debt, to cover cases where the pawn's value exceeds the debt.
This ensures accuracy when the value is greater than the debt.

كتاب الرهن مسيرة ثلاثة أيام فإن الجعل على الراهن وعلى المرتهن نصفان لأن العبد نصفه مضمون بالدين ونصفه أمانة فيكون الجعل بينهما بالحصص. ومنها مداواة الأمراض والجروح لأنه ينقسم ذلك على المضمون وعلى الأمانة بالحصص وما أصاب المضمون فعلى المرتهن، وأما أصاب الأمانة فعلى الراهن. ولو قال وهو مضمون بالأقل من قيمة المضمون ومن الدين لكان أولى ليشمل ما إذا كان قيمة المرهون أكثر من الدين في الأصل والباطل من الرهن ما لا يكون منعقداً أصلاً كالباطل من البيع والفاسد ما يكون منعقداً لكن بوصف الفساد والمقابل به يكون مالاً مضموناً، وفي كل موضع لم يكن الرهن مالاً ولم يكن المقابل به مضموناً لا ينعقد الرهن أصلاً وهو الباطل، وتعتبر قيمة الرهن يوم القبض ولم يذكر المؤلف أحكام غلبة الماء على المرهونة قال في المحيط أرض مرهونة غلب عليها الماء بمنزلة العبد إذا أبق لأنهار بما ينزل عنها الماء فتكون الأرض منتفعاً بها فلا يسقط الدين لاحتمال العود كالآبق. ولو عبداً حلال الدم أو سرق عند الراهن فقطع عند المرتهن فذاك من ضمان الراهن ولم رهن يذهب من الدين شيء وبقي مرتهناً بجميع الدين عند الإمام، وعندهما السرقة عيب ويقوم سارقاً وحلال الدم وغير سارق وغير حلال الدم فيسقط من الدين بمقدار قيمته حلال الدم والقطع ويكون رهناً بحصة قيمته كذلك، ولو وجب عليه حد القذف أو الزنا عند المرتهن أو دخله عيب فيسقط من الدين بقدره رهن ثوباً يساوي خمسة دراهم ومثال ذهب يساري عشرة بخمسة دراهم فهلك الذهب وليس الثوب حتى الخرق ضمن قيمة الثوب يحسب ماله ذلك درهم وثلثان لأنه ذهب بإذهاب الذهب ثلثا الدين وذلك ثلاثة دراهم وثلث درهم من الأرض قال لأن بإزاء الذهب ثلث الدين وبإزاء الثوب ثلثه ، فإذا ذهب الذهب واستهلك الثوب يذهب بإذهاب الثوب ثلث الدين ويضمن مثقال الذهب فيكون رهناً عنده بثلاثة دراهم وثلث . وذكر المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى حكم هلاك العين المرهونة في يد المرتهن ولم يذكر حكم نقصانها في الخلاصة : إذا نقصت العين المرهونة في يد المرتهن إن كان النقصان في عينها سقط من ولم يتعرض لما إذا كان بالدين رهنان من جهتين مختلفتين قال قاضيخان : الدين بقدره ا هـ . رجل عليه دين لآخر وبه كفيل فأخذ الطالب من الكفيل رهناً ومن الأصيل رهناً وأحدهما الآخر وبكل واحد وفاء بالدين فهلك أحد الرهنين عند المرتهن قال زفر رحمه الله تعالى : بعد أيهما هلك بكل الدين. وقال الإمام أبو يوسف رحمه الله تعالى : إذا هلك الرهن الثاني؛ فإن كان الراهن علم بالرهن الأول فإن الثاني يهلك بنصف الدين وإن لم يعلم بذلك يهلك بجميع الدين. وذكر في كتاب الرهن أن الثاني يهلك بنصف الدين ولم يذكر العلم والجهل وهو الصحيح لأن كل واحد منهما يطالب بجميع الدين فيجعل الرهن الثاني زيادة في الرهن الأول، فإن كانت قيمتهما سواء قسم الدين عليهما فالثاني إذا هلك يهلك بنصف الدين وقد قالوا : لو شرط إذا ضاع يكون مجاناً فالشرط باطل ويهلك بالدين ولم يتعرض لما إذا هلك
Chapter of Pledge. If the journey is three days, then the wage is half on the pledger and half on the pledgee, because the slave is half guaranteed by the debt and half a trust, so the wage is between them proportionally. Among these is treating illnesses and wounds, because that is divided between the guaranteed part and the trust part proportionally; what affects the guaranteed part is upon the pledgee, and what affects the trust part is upon the pledger. It would be better if he said: it is guaranteed by the lesser of the value of the guaranteed part and the debt, to include when the value of the pawned item is more than the debt originally. The invalid (bāṭil) of pledge is that which is not contracted at all, like the invalid of sale; the defective (fāsid) is that which is contracted but with a description of defectiveness, and its counterpart is guaranteed property. In every case where the pledge is not property and the counterpart is not guaranteed, the pledge does not contract at all—that is the invalid. The value of the pledge is considered on the day of possession. The author did not mention the rulings of water overwhelming the pawned item. It is said in al-Muḥīṭ: A mortgaged land that is overcome by water is like a slave who has run away, because the water may recede from it, so the land can be benefited from; thus the debt is not dropped due to the possibility of its return, like the runaway. If the slave is of permissible blood (ḥalāl al-dam) or stolen at the pledger's, then cut at the pledgee's, that is from the pledger's guarantee, and nothing goes from the debt, and he remains a pledgee for the entire debt according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). According to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), theft is a defect, and it is valued as a thief and of permissible blood, and as non-thief and non-permissible blood; thus an amount equal to its value when of permissible blood and the cut is deducted from the debt, and it becomes a pledge for the share of its value likewise. If the ḥadd penalty for slander or adultery is due upon him at the pledgee's, or a defect enters it, then an amount equal to it is deducted from the debt. A man pawned a cloth worth five dirhams and as an example gold with ten dirhams' silver for five dirhams; the gold perished, and the cloth was not even a rag; he guarantees the value of the cloth, calculating that he has one and two-thirds dirhams, because the gold's removal took two-thirds of the debt, which is three and one-third dirhams from the land. He said: because against the gold is one-third of the debt, and against the cloth is one-third; so if the gold perishes and the cloth is consumed, the removal of the cloth takes one-third of the debt, and he guarantees the mithqāl of the gold, so it becomes a pledge with him for three and one-third dirhams. The author, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned the ruling of the destruction of the pawned item in the hands of the pledgee, but did not mention the ruling of its deficiency. In al-Khulāṣa: If the pawned item decreases in the hands of the pledgee, if the decrease is in its substance, something is deducted from the debt. He did not address when there are two pawns for the same debt from different parties. Qāḍīkhān said: the debt is proportional. A man owes a debt to another and has a surety (kafīl); the claimant took one pawn from the surety and one from the principal debtor, both equal, and each covers the debt. One of the two pawns perishes with the pledgee. Zufar said: whichever perishes, it perishes with the entire debt. Imam Abu Yusuf said: if the second pawn perishes, if the pledger knew of the first pawn, then the second perishes with half the debt; if he did not know, it perishes with the entire debt. It is mentioned in Kitāb al-Rahn that the second perishes with half the debt, and it did not mention knowledge and ignorance, and that is correct, because each of them is demanded for the entire debt, so the second pawn is considered an increase over the first. If their values are equal, the debt is divided between them; so if the second perishes, it perishes with half the debt. They said: if he stipulates that if it is lost it is gratis, the condition is void, and it perishes with the debt. And he did not address what if it perishes.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage from al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqāʾiq covering multiple rulings on pledge (rahn). The text first discusses the expenses for returning a runaway slave: if the journey is three days, the wage is split equally between pledger and pledgee because the slave is partly guaranteed (for the debt) and partly a trust. Medical treatment is also divided proportionally. The author suggests a more precise definition: the pledge is guaranteed up to the lesser of the value of the guaranteed portion and the debt. Then the distinction between bāṭil (void) and fāsid (defective) pledge is clarified: bāṭil is not contracted at all, like a void sale; fāsid is contracted but with a defect, and the counterpart is guaranteed property. The value of the pledge is reckoned at the time of possession. The ruling on land overtaken by water is analogous to a runaway slave—the debt is not dropped because the water may recede. Next, a scenario where a slave with permissible blood (ḥalāl al-dam) or stolen is cut—according to Abu Hanifa, the pledgee remains entitled to the full debt; according to the two disciples, the theft is a defect, and the pledgee's claim is reduced proportionally. Similarly, if a ḥadd penalty becomes due, the debt is reduced. A numerical example illustrates how the loss is computed when multiple items are pawned. The passage then addresses a case with two pawns from different parties (the surety and the principal debtor). Zufar holds that whichever pawn perishes covers the entire debt; Abu Yusuf distinguishes based on the pledger's knowledge; the relied-upon ruling (ṣaḥīḥ) is that the second pawn perishes with half the debt if values are equal, treating it as an increase. Finally, a stipulation that loss is gratis is void, and the pawn still covers the debt.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract where property is given as security for a debt, held by the creditor until debt repayment.
مضمون (maḍmūn): Guaranteed; the portion of the pawn corresponding to the debt amount, for which the pledgee bears responsibility.
أمانة (amāna): Trust; the excess value of the pawn beyond the debt, for which the pledger bears responsibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave worth 150 is pawned for a debt of 100. The slave falls ill and requires 50 for medicine. How much does the pledger pay?
A) 50
B) 33.33
C) 0
D) 16.67

Key Takeaway
Expenses for a pawned item are shared proportionally based on the split between debt guarantee and trust.

Bāṭil and Fāsid in Pledge: Key Distinction
Definition of bāṭil and fāsid in pledge with valuation rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a bāṭil (void) and fāsid (defective) pledge contract and state the consequence for the counterpart being guaranteed.

Core Idea
A bāṭil pledge is void ab initio; a fāsid pledge is contracted but defective, with the counterpart being guaranteed property.

This passage defines the categories of invalid pledge: bāṭil (void) and fāsid (defective). The distinction is crucial for determining whether the pledgee has any claim on the property. The author also notes that the value of the pledge is assessed at the time of possession.

Guided Path
1
Understand bāṭil pledge
A pledge is bāṭil when it is not contracted at all, analogous to a void sale. This occurs when the pledged item is not considered property in Islamic law, or when the counterpart (the thing being secured) is not guaranteed.
Bāṭil pledge has no legal effect.
2
Understand fāsid pledge
A pledge is fāsid when it is contracted (therefore it exists) but has a defect in its description (e.g., missing a condition). The counterpart in a fāsid pledge is considered guaranteed property (mālin maḍmūn).
Fāsid can be rectified; it is not totally void.
3
Identify the ruling on valuation
The value of the pawn is considered on the day of possession (yawm al-qabḍ), not at the time of contract or later.
This is important for determining the extent of guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرهن مسيرة ثلاثة أيام فإن الجعل على الراهن وعلى المرتهن نصفان لأن العبد نصفه مضمون بالدين ونصفه أمانة فيكون الجعل بينهما بالحصص. ومنها مداواة الأمراض والجروح لأنه ينقسم ذلك على المضمون وعلى الأمانة بالحصص وما أصاب المضمون فعلى المرتهن، وأما أصاب الأمانة فعلى الراهن. ولو قال وهو مضمون بالأقل من قيمة المضمون ومن الدين لكان أولى ليشمل ما إذا كان قيمة المرهون أكثر من الدين في الأصل والباطل من الرهن ما لا يكون منعقداً أصلاً كالباطل من البيع والفاسد ما يكون منعقداً لكن بوصف الفساد والمقابل به يكون مالاً مضموناً، وفي كل موضع لم يكن الرهن مالاً ولم يكن المقابل به مضموناً لا ينعقد الرهن Key Terms
باطل (bāṭil): A contract that is void ab initio, not contracted at all.
فاسد (fāsid): A contract that is contracted but with a defect; the counterpart is guaranteed property.

What distinguishes a fāsid pledge from a bāṭil pledge in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Bāṭil involves property; fāsid involves services
B) Fāsid is contracted with defect; bāṭil is not contracted at all
C) Bāṭil requires a condition; fāsid does not
D) Fāsid and bāṭil are the same

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, fāsid pledge is a valid contract with a defect; bāṭil pledge is void.

Water Overflow on Mortgaged Land: Debt Not Discharged
Ruling on pawned land overtaken by water, analogy to runaway slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling that temporary loss of benefit from pawned land (due to water overflow) does not discharge the debt, by analogy to a runaway slave.

Core Idea
If pawned land is overwhelmed by water but the water may recede, the debt remains due, like a runaway slave that may return.

The author notes that al-Marghinani did not discuss the case of pawned land overtaken by water. He then cites al-Muḥīṭ: such land is like a runaway slave; since the water may recede and the land become productive again, the debt is not discharged. This ruling illustrates how temporary incapacity does not extinguish the obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation
A mortgaged land is flooded by water, rendering it temporarily useless. The question: does the debt get reduced or discharged?
2
Apply the analogy (qiyas) to runaway slave
Just as a runaway slave may return and still serve as security, the land may be benefited from again after the water recedes. Therefore, the debt remains intact.
The value of the pawn is not permanently lost.
3
Derive the ruling
The debt is not dropped; the land remains a pawn. The creditor's right to the land continues.
This is contrary to permanent loss where the debt would be discharged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
