Guarantee of Pledge After Mutual Denial of Debt
Ruling on guarantee of pledge when both agree no debt existed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on whether a pledge remains guaranteed when both parties deny the debt before its perishing, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Two Hanafi views: mutual denial may remove guarantee (amanah) or leave it (daman) because conjecture of debt persists.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about the status of a pledge after the pledgor and pledgee jointly affirm that no debt existed. The ruling turns on the timing of the denial and the role of legal conjecture (tawahhum). Two groups of later Hanafi scholars offer opposing reasoning, each with practical consequences for the pledgee's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two temporal scenarios
First, the parties agree no debt after the pledge perished: the perishing is treated as constructive receipt, so pledgee must return the debt. Second, they agree no debt before perishing: here the mashayikh differ.
The first scenario is undisputed; the dispute concerns only the second.
2
State the first view (amanah)
Some say the pledge becomes a trust (amanah) because the guarantee was based on a conjectured debt; when the conjecture is removed by mutual denial, the guarantee falls.
Analogized to remission (ibra) or gift (hiba) removing debt.
3
State the second view (daman)
Others say the guarantee persists because the conjecture of debt is not necessarily removed by mutual denial—they might later recall a debt.
Conjecture remains possible; thus the pledge remains guaranteed.
4
Analyze the illa (effective cause) of the dispute
The core issue is whether tawahhum (conjecture) of debt is eliminated by tawafuq (mutual agreement) on nonexistence, or whether it can survive because of future possibility.
This is a rational (dalil aqli) argument.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
If the pledge is destroyed, in the first view the pledgee owes nothing; in the second view he must compensate the value.
The practical difference is liability for loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصادق الراهن والمرتهن أن لا دين بعد أن اتفقا أنه ألف وهلك الرهن فعلى المرتهن أن يرد الألف لأن الرهن حين هلك كان مضموناً بالدين لأنهما لم يتصادقا أن لا قبل الهلاك فصار المرتهن مستوفياً للدين حكماً بالهلاك فصار كما لو استوفاه حقيقة، ولو تصادقا أن لا دين قبل الهلاك اختلف المشايخ فيه قيل يملك أمانة لأن الرهن حصل بدين مضمون يتوهم وجوبه فصار الرهن مضموناً بدين مظنون، فإذا زال التوهم بالتصادق على أن لا دين يزول الضمان كما لو زال بالإبراء والهبة وقيل يضمن لأنه توهم وجوب الدين لم يزل تصادقهما على أن لا دين لأن تصادقهما على عدم الدين لا يمنعهما عن التصادق على الوجوب بعد ذلك لجواز أن يتذكرا بعد ما تصادقا أنه كان عليه دين وإن بقي توهم الوجوب بقي مضموناً عليه لأن ما به يثبت الضمان وهو توهم الاقتراض منه في الثاني بامتناعه الاقتراض لم يزل الجواز أن يكون أقرضه بعد ذلك فيكون مضموناً عليه.
If the pledgor and pledgee mutually affirm that there is no debt, after they had agreed it was one thousand and the pledge perished, then the pledgee must return the one thousand because when the pledge perished it was guaranteed by the debt since they did not mutually affirm its nonexistence before the perishing. Thus the pledgee became effectively in receipt of the debt by the perishing, like actual receipt. But if they mutually affirmed no debt before the perishing, the mashayikh differed: some said he holds as amanah because the pledge was obtained for a debt whose obligation was supposed, so the pledge became guaranteed by a conjectural debt; when the supposition is removed by mutual affirmation of no debt, the guarantee is removed just as by remission or gift. Others said he is liable because the supposition of debt obligation does not cease by their mutual affirmation of no debt, for their affirmation of nonexistence does not prevent them from affirming existence later; it is possible they recall after affirming that there was a debt. If the supposition of obligation remains, the guarantee remains, because that which establishes the guarantee—namely the supposition he borrowed from him in the second instance by his refraining from borrowing—is not removed; it is possible he lent him afterwards, so he is liable.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where two parties initially agree they had a debt of one thousand and the pledge perished, but later they both say there was no debt. The author presents two rulings based on timing. If they affirm no debt after the pledge perished, the pledge was already guaranteed by the debt at the time of perishing, so the perishing is treated as constructive receipt of the debt; hence the pledgee must return the one thousand. If they affirm no debt before the perishing, there is disagreement among later Hanafi scholars. One view: the pledge becomes an amanah (trust) because the debt was only conjectural, so with the conjecture gone, guarantee falls. The other view: the pledge remains guaranteed because the conjecture of debt may persist; the parties might later recall a debt, so the guarantee continues. The author presents both views without explicit tarjih here.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge or collateral held by the creditor as security for a debt.
مضمون (madmun): Guaranteed; the pledge is liable to be used to satisfy the debt if it perishes.
أمانة (amanah): Trust; the pledge is held in trust and not liable for loss unless negligence.
استيفاء حكمي (istifa' hukmi): Constructive receipt; the pledgee is deemed to have received the debt by the perishing of the pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario, where the pledgor and pledgee mutually affirm no debt after the pledge perished, what is the ruling on the pledgee?
A) He must return the pledge itself or its value.
B) He must return the amount of the debt (one thousand) because the perishing is deemed constructive receipt.
C) He does nothing; the pledge is a trust and no compensation is due.
D) He keeps the pledge as his own property.

Key Takeaway
When both parties deny a debt before the pledge perishes, Hanafi scholars differ: some consider the pledge a trust, others maintain its guarantee due to possible later acknowledgment.

Pledge Taken on Promise of Loan
Ruling on pledge perish when taken for promised loan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a pledge perishes after being taken on the promise of a loan, and identify the legal basis using the Hanafi principle of 'jihah al-rahn'.

Core Idea
A pledge taken on promise of a loan is like a real pledge – possession alone creates liability.

In this lesson, we examine a specific case from the Hanafi fiqh of rahn (pledge). A person gives a pledge (a slave) to a creditor on the understanding that the creditor will lend him a sum. If the pledge perishes before the loan is given, what is the creditor's liability? The passage clarifies that the possession with the intention of pledge (jihah al-rahn) suffices to make the creditor a bailee – and if the pledge is lost, he must compensate its value, limited by the promised loan amount.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
Recognize the scenario: a pledge is taken on the promise of a future loan, and the pledge perishes.
The pledge is given with the understanding that the creditor will lend a specified amount.
2
Note the condition for liability
The text specifies 'if its value is less than one thousand' – the creditor guarantees the value only up to the promised loan amount.
If the value equals or exceeds the promise, the ruling may differ (not specified here).
3
Apply the ruling
The creditor must compensate the value of the pledge (if less than the promised loan).
This is because the pledge is considered validly taken by way of pledge (jihah al-rahn).
4
Understand the reasoning via qiyas
The author analogizes to purchase: possession with the intention of a thing (here, pledge) creates legal effects just as possession with intention to purchase creates ownership.
Possession by way of pledge (jihah al-rahn) is thus equivalent to actual pledge possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك لو أخذ عبداً على أن يقرضه ألفاً هلك العبد، فإن كانت قيمته أقل من ألف ضمن قيمته لأنه بجهة الرهن مقبوض فصار كالمقبوض بحقيقة الرهن لأن المقبوض على جهة الشيء كالمقبوض على الشراء.
Similarly, if someone took a slave on the basis that he would lend him one thousand, and the slave perished: if its value is less than one thousand, he guarantees its value because it is possessed by way of pledge (rahn), so it becomes like that possessed by actual rahn, for what is possessed by way of a thing is like what is possessed for purchase.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for a case where a person gives a pledge (a slave) with the understanding that the creditor will lend him one thousand. The pledge is taken as security for a future loan. If the slave perishes while in the creditor's hand, the creditor is liable for its value, but only up to the amount of the promised loan. The reasoning is that possession by way of pledge (even for a promise) makes it like a real pledge. The analogy is made with a purchase: if someone takes possession of an item with the intention to purchase, it is treated as a purchase. Similarly, possession with the intention of pledge makes it a pledge.

Key Terms
جهة الرهن (jihah al-rahn): Possession by way of pledge (i.e., with the intention that it be a pledge).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone takes a slave as pledge for a promised loan of 1000, and the slave perishes when its value is 800, what does the creditor owe?
A) Nothing, because the loan was not yet given.
B) The value of the slave, which is 800.
C) The full promised loan amount of 1000.
D) The difference between the value and the loan, i.e., 200.

Key Takeaway
A pledge taken on promise of loan becomes a valid pledge by possession alone, and the creditor is liable for its value if it perishes, up to the promised loan amount.

Detaining the Pledge After Rescinding a Salam Contract
Ruling on right to hold pledge after rescinding salam contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the right to detain a pledge after mutual rescission of a salam contract and distinguish it from remission of debt.

Core Idea
After rescinding a salam contract, the buyer may keep the pledge until the capital is returned.

In a salam contract, the buyer pays the full price upfront for future delivery of a commodity. Often a pledge is taken as security. If both parties agree to rescind, does the buyer lose the right to hold the pledge? The Hanafi ruling clarifies that the pledge remains valid until the capital is returned, based on the nature of the guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage describes a salam for food, where a pledge was taken. Then the parties mutually rescind (tafasukh). Determine the legal effect on the pledge.
2
State the ruling on detaining the pledge
The buyer may detain the pledge until he receives the capital back. The reason: the capital stands for the commodity, so the pledge is for the capital.
3
Analyze the consequence if the pledge perishes
If the pledge perishes in the buyer's hand before capital is returned, it perishes as guaranteed by the food (the commodity), meaning the buyer still has a claim for the food. The guarantee of the food remains because rescission does not cancel the obligation to deliver the food until capital is returned.
4
Contrast with ibra' (remission)
If the seller released the buyer from the debt (ibra'), the guarantee would fall entirely because the debt itself disappears. Here, the debt (food) does not disappear until capital is returned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أسلم في طعام وأخذ به رهناً ثم تفاسخا العقد كان له أن يحبس الرهن سوم حتى يقبض برأس المال لأن رأس المال يدل على المسلم فيه فظهر أن الرهن في حق البدل، فإن هلك الرهن في يده هلك بالطعام لأنه كان مضموناً بالطعام وبالفسخ لم يسقط الطعام أصلاً ما لم يصل إليه رأس المال فبقي مضموناً به كما كان بخلاف ما لو أبرأه عن الدين لأن هناك سقط الضمان أصلاً لسقوط الدين أصلاً.
And if one made a salam contract for food and took a pledge for it, then they rescinded the contract: he may detain the pledge until he receives the capital, because the capital indicates the commodity (muslam fih), so it becomes clear that the pledge is for the substitute (badal). If the pledge perishes in his hand, it perishes as guaranteed by the food, because it was guaranteed by the food, and by rescission the food does not drop as long as the capital has not reached him, so it remains guaranteed by it as before, contrary to if he had released him from the debt, because there the guarantee falls entirely due to the dropping of the debt entirely.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage discusses a salam contract (advance payment for future delivery of a commodity). The buyer (rabb al-salam) pays the capital and takes a pledge for the commodity due. If they mutually rescind the contract (tafasukha), the buyer may keep the pledge until the seller returns the capital. The reasoning: the capital stands for the commodity; the pledge was taken for the commodity, and after rescission, the obligation of the commodity remains until capital is returned. If the pledge perishes before capital is returned, it is treated as having been received for the commodity, so the buyer loses the pledge and still has claim to the commodity (or its equivalent). This differs from remission (ibra') of the debt, where guarantee ends because the debt itself ends. Here, the debt (commodity) does not end until capital is returned.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A sale where the price is paid in advance and the goods are delivered later.
تفاسخ (tafasukh): Mutual rescission of a contract by agreement of both parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract, after tafasukh (rescission), if the pledge perishes before the capital is returned, the pledge is considered:
A) A trust (amanah) and the buyer loses nothing.
B) As having been received for the food (muslam fih), so the buyer still has claim to the food.
C) As having been received for the capital, so the buyer loses the capital.
D) The seller must pay the value of the pledge to the buyer.

Key Takeaway
After mutual rescission of a salam contract, the buyer retains the right to hold the pledge until the capital is returned; the pledge remains guaranteed by the commodity until then.

Detaining Pledge After Mutual Rescission
Ruling on buyer's right to hold pledge after iqalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on detaining a pledge after iqalah and explain the analogy from the salam case.

Core Idea
After iqalah, the pledge may be held until the counter-obligation is returned; if it perishes, it satisfies the price.

In this passage, the author extends the earlier ruling on pledges in salam contracts to a symmetric scenario: a buyer who gave a pledge for the price and took possession of the sold item. When the sale is rescinded by mutual consent (iqalah), the roles reverse, and the original buyer (now seller of the item) may detain the pledge until he receives back the item he has already taken. This lesson explores the ruling and its underlying analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Consider a sale where the buyer takes delivery of the sold item in advance and gives a pledge (rahn) for the deferred price. Later, both parties agree to rescind the sale (iqalah).
This is the reverse of the salam case where the seller gave a pledge for the advance payment.
2
State the Ruling
The original buyer (now obligated to return the sold item) may detain the pledge until he receives the sold item back. This is a right to hold the pledge until the counter-obligation is fulfilled.
The text says 'له أن يحبس الرهن حتى يقبض المبيع'.
3
Identify the Analogy
The author refers back to the earlier salam case (as previously explained). In that case, the seller gave a pledge for the advance payment and then iqalah occurred; the seller could hold the pledge until repayment. The same principle applies here, but with roles swapped.
The argument type is qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Analyze the Consequence of Loss
If the pledge perishes in the holder's possession (the original buyer), it is deemed as having been received for the price (halka bil-thaman). This means the obligation to return the price is satisfied to the extent of the pledge's value.
This follows the same rule as in the salam case: the pledge serves as security and, if lost, compensates the debt.
5
Apply the Principle
The general principle is: after iqalah, the party who has received a counter-value before rescission may hold any pledge given for that counter-value until the counter-value is returned.
This ensures that rescission does not leave one party unprotected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك لو أسلم المبيع وأخذ بالثمن رهناً ثم تقايلا كان له أن يحبس الرهن حتى يقبض المبيع فإن هلك الرهن في يده هلك بالثمن على ما بينا.
Similarly, if he gave the sold item in advance and took a pledge for the price, then they mutually rescinded (taqayala), he may detain the pledge until he receives the sold item. If the pledge perishes in his hand, it perishes as guaranteed by the price, as we have explained.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This is a parallel to the previous case but from the seller's perspective. In a sale, the buyer might take the sold item (mubayai') in advance and give a pledge for the deferred price. If they later mutually rescind (qila'), the roles reverse: the original buyer becomes the seller of the item he already received. He may hold the pledge (which he originally gave) until he returns the sold item. If the pledge perishes, it is deemed received for the price, meaning he still has the price due from him? The text says 'هلك بالثمن' meaning it perishes as guaranteed by the price. The explanation is brief, referring back to the earlier reasoning in the salam case.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Mutual rescission of a sale by request of the buyer and acceptance of the seller.
رهن (rahn): A pledge given as security for a debt, which can be detained by the creditor until the debt is paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After iqalah of a sale where the buyer had given a pledge and taken delivery of the sold item, what can the buyer (now seller of the item) do with the pledge?
A) He must return it immediately.
B) He may detain it until he receives the sold item back.
C) He may keep it as compensation for the price.
D) He must sell it and return the proceeds.

Key Takeaway
After iqalah, the party who had given a pledge for a counter-value they have already received may detain that pledge until the counter-value is returned, ensuring balance after rescission.

Pledge in Salam and Compromise on Capital: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Ruling on pledge after compromise (sulh) on capital in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school applies istihsan over qiyas in a salam pledge scenario and derive the ruling that the pledge remains valid for the new debt.

Core Idea
When a pledge's original debt is replaced, istihsan keeps the pledge alive by treating the substitute as standing in the same place.

In a salam contract, the buyer often takes a pledge to secure delivery of the commodity. If later they compromise (sulh) to cancel the contract and refund the capital, what happens to that pledge? Strict qiyas (analogy) would void it because the capital is a new debt. However, the Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to uphold the pledge, treating the capital as a substitute that stands in the place of the original commodity. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind both views and the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the opening lines: a person pays 500 in salam for food and takes a slave as pledge, then makes a compromise (sulh) to recover the capital instead of the food. Understand that the original debt (food) is replaced by the capital.
The key is that the debt changes from food to capital.
2
State the two possible rulings
Note that the text presents two rulings: by qiyas the pledge does not transfer to the capital (so the pledgor cannot retrieve the slave and the capital remains a debt); by istihsan the pledge becomes a pledge for the capital and is guaranteed.
These are not positions of different schools but two methods within Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the basis of qiyas
Read the 'wajh al-qiyas': the capital is not the muslam fih (food) in reality or law because it is not a substitute; the food was obligated by the original contract, while the capital is obligated by the iqalah (cancellation). They are opposites, so a pledge for one cannot cover the other.
The qiyas reasoning focuses on the distinct legal identities of the two obligations.
4
Analyze the basis of istihsan
Read the 'wajh al-istihsan': the capital stands in the place of the muslam fih because it was the original substitute in the contract, and when the right to the food fell by iqalah, the right reverted to its substitute. Thus the pledge for the food becomes a pledge for what stands in its place, just as a pledge for a usurped item becomes a pledge for its value.
The key analogy: a pledge for an item that is lost becomes a pledge for its value – similarly, the capital is the 'value' of the food here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أسلم خمسمائة في طعام فرهن به عبداً يساوي الطعام وقبضه ثم صالح على رأس المال فالقياس أن لا يقبض الراهن العبد ورأس المال دين عليه . الاستحسان يجعل رهناً بدينه ويكون مضموناً. وجه القياس أن رأس المال غير المسلم فيه حقيقة وحكماً لأنه ليس ببدل عن الطعام لأن الطعام وجب بالعقد ورأس المال وجب بالإقالة وهما ضدان فما وجب بأحدهما لا . بدلاً عن الآخر فالرهن بالطعام لا يكون رهناً. وجه الاستحسان رأس المال بدل عن المسلم فيه قائم مقامه لأنه كان بدلاً له في العقد وبالإقالة والصلح لما سقط حقه في المسلم فيه عاد حقه إلى بدله لأنه وإن كان ديناً حادثاً لكن لما قام مقام المسلم إثباتاً وإسقاطاً فالرهن بالمسلم فيه يكون رهناً بما قام مقامه كالرهن بالمغصوب رهن بقيمته لأنها قائمة ،مقامه فإذا استوفى رأس المال ثم هلك عنده العبد من غير صنع يعطيه المرتهن مثل الطعام الذي كان له على المسلم إليه ويأخذ منه رأس المال.
He advanced five hundred in salam for food and took a slave as pledge equal to the food and possessed it. Then he made a compromise (sulah) on the capital. By qiyas, the pledgor would not receive the slave, and the capital remains a debt against him. By istihsan, it is made a pledge for his debt and becomes guaranteed. Basis of qiyas: the capital is not the muslam fih (the commodity) in reality and in law, because it is not a substitute for the food; the food was obligated by the contract, while the capital was obligated by the iqalah, and they are opposites. So what is obligated by one cannot be a substitute for the other; thus the pledge for the food does not become a pledge. Basis of istihsan: the capital is a substitute for the muslam fih, standing in its place, because it was a substitute for it in the contract, and by iqalah and sulh, when his right in the muslam fih fell, his right reverted to its substitute. Although it is a new debt, since it stands in place of the muslam fih in affirmation and negation, the pledge for the muslam fih becomes a pledge for what stands in its place, like a pledge for a usurped item is a pledge for its value because it stands in its place. So if he receives the capital and then the slave perishes in his hand without his fault, the pledgee gives him food like that which he had against the muslam ilayh and takes the capital from him.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage presents a scenario in salam: the buyer (rabb al-salam) pays 500 in advance and takes a pledge for the food. Later, they compromise (sulh) such that the buyer takes back his capital instead of the food. The qiyas (analogy) says the pledge for the food does not transfer to the capital, so the pledgor cannot get the pledge back; the capital remains a debt. But istihsan (juristic preference) rules that the pledge is now for the capital, because the capital stands in the place of the food. The reasoning: the capital was the original substitute; after iqalah, the right to the food is replaced by the capital. Just as a pledge for a usurped item becomes a pledge for its value, here too the pledge for the food becomes a pledge for the capital. The practical outcome: if the buyer receives the capital and then the pledge perishes in his hand, he must give the seller (muslam ilayh) food equivalent to that originally owed, and the seller returns the capital. So the pledge is treated as having been received for the capital.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; preferring one analogical outcome over another due to equity or need.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause (illa).
إقالة (iqalah): Mutual rescission of a contract; cancellation with the consent of both parties.
صلح (sulh): Compromise; a settlement that ends a dispute or modifies contractual terms.
مسلم فيه (muslam fih): The commodity to be delivered in a salam contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the salam scenario, after compromise on the capital, what is the status of the original pledge according to istihsan?
A) It becomes a pledge for the new debt (capital) and is guaranteed.
B) It is no longer a pledge and must be returned to the pledgor.
C) It remains a pledge for the food, not for the capital.
D) It is void because the original debt is gone.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a pledge's original debt is replaced by its substitute (as in salam compromise), istihsan keeps the pledge valid because the substitute stands in the place of the original.

Pledge for Loan of Wheat Then Compromise on Barley
Ruling on pledge when loan of wheat is compromised for specific barley

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a loan of wheat secured by a pledge is compromised for specific barley, and explain why the pledge remains guaranteed by the original wheat debt.

Core Idea
A compromise on a debt does not shift the pledge's security; it is treated like actual fulfillment.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with a specific scenario: a loan of wheat with a pledge, followed by a compromise (sulh) where the debtor agrees to give a specific quantity of barley instead. The key question is: what happens to the pledge? The author explains that the compromise is analogous to actual fulfillment of the original debt, so the pledge continues to secure the original wheat, not the barley. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi concept of constructive receipt (istifa' hukmi) and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and the ruling
The scenario: A lends B one kurr of wheat and takes a garment as pledge (rahn). Then B compromises (sulh) by promising a specific kurr of barley. The Hanafi ruling: the garment remains a pledge for the barley? Actually, the text says it becomes a pledge for the barley (?), but then clarifies that if it perishes, it perishes as guaranteed by the wheat. So the ruling is that the pledge secures the original debt (wheat), not the new promise (barley).
The Arabic: 'ويصير الثوب رهناً بالشعير' means the garment becomes a pledge for the barley, but the subsequent ruling shows that the guarantee is attached to the wheat.
2
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The author reasons that when the pledge perishes, it is 'madhmunan bil-hintah' (guaranteed by the wheat) because the compromise releases the debtor from the wheat, making it as if the wheat was actually received (istifa' haqiqi). This is the key: compromise is treated like fulfillment.
The text: 'لأنه برئ من الحنطة فصار كما لو برئ بالإيفاء'.
3
Compare with actual receipt in salam
The author contrasts with a salam contract: if the muslam fih (the commodity) was actually received and then the parties mutually rescind (iqalah), the recipient must return similar goods and take back his capital. Similarly, here the compromise after 'constructive receipt' (istifa' hukmi) has the same effect: the pledge perishing is deemed receipt of the wheat.
This comparison illustrates that constructive receipt cannot exceed actual receipt; thus, the ruling parallels the actual case.
4
Distinguish the invalid case
The passage notes that if the lender actually received the wheat first (actual receipt), then a compromise on barley is invalid because there is no debt of barley. The same applies after constructive receipt: the compromise is only valid if it substitutes an existing debt, not creates a new one.
This reinforces that the compromise does not create a new debt; it merely settles the original.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقرض رجلاً كر حنطة وارتهن منه ثوباً قيمته أو صالحه من عليه الحنطة على كر شعير بعينه ويصير الثوب رهناً بالشعير، فإذا هلك يهلك مضموناً بالحنطة لأنه . برى عن الحنطة فصار كما لو برئ بالإيفاء ويجوز أن يكون الشيء رهناً ولا يكون مضموناً كزوائد الرهن يكون محبوساً ولا يكون مضموناً، وذلك لأن الرهن استيفاء حكمي والاستيفاء الحكمي لا يربو على الاستيفاء الحقيقي. ولو استوفى المسلم فيه حقيقة ثم تقايلا السلم صحت الإقالة ويرد عليه طعاماً مثله ويأخذ رأس ماله فكذا إذا اصطلحا يعد الاستيفاء الحكمي. وفي مسألة القرض لو صالحه على الشعير بعدما استوفى الحنطة حقيقة لم يجز الصالح لأنه لو صالحه على دين وليس عليه ذلك الدين يصح أصلاً فكذا إذا اصطلحا بعد الاستيفاء الحكمي.
A man lent another a kurr of wheat and took a pledge of a garment of equal value, or he made a compromise with the one who owed the wheat for a specific kurr of barley; then the garment becomes a pledge for the barley. If it perishes, it perishes as guaranteed by the wheat because he is released from the wheat, so it becomes as if he were released by fulfillment. And it is permissible that a thing be a pledge and not be guaranteed, like the increases of a pledge are detained but not guaranteed. That is because the pledge is constructive receipt (istifa' hukmi), and constructive receipt does not exceed actual receipt. If he actually received the muslam fih and then they mutually rescinded the salam, the iqalah is valid; he returns food similar to it and takes his capital. So too when they compromise after constructive receipt. And in the loan case, if he compromised on the barley after he actually received the wheat, the compromise is not valid, because if he compromises on a debt that is not owed, it is not valid at all; similarly if they compromise after constructive receipt.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage discusses a loan of wheat with a pledge. If the debtor compromises by giving a specific quantity of barley instead of the wheat, the pledge (garment) remains secured for the wheat, not the barley. The reasoning: the debtor is released from the wheat obligation by the compromise, which is treated like actual fulfillment. The pledge perishes as guaranteed by the wheat, meaning its destruction is deemed receipt of the wheat. An important distinction: the pledge itself is not guaranteed (like its increase) because it is constructive receipt. The text contrasts with salam: if the muslam fih was actually received, then iqalah is valid and the recipient must return similar food and take back his capital. But in the loan case, if the lender actually received the wheat first, then compromising on barley is invalid because there is no debt of barley. This applies even after constructive receipt (like the pledge being deemed receipt).

Key Terms
استيفاء حكمي (istifa' hukmi): Constructive receipt; the pledgee is deemed to have received the debt by the perishing of the pledge.
صالح (salaha): He made a compromise (sulh); a transaction to settle a dispute or debt on different terms.
مضموناً (madmunan): Guaranteed; the pledge is guaranteed by the debt, meaning it is liable for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a lender takes a pledge for a loan of wheat, then the borrower compromises by giving barley, and the pledge perishes, what is the ruling?
A) The pledge perishes as guaranteed by the barley.
B) The pledge perishes as guaranteed by the wheat, because the compromise is treated like fulfillment.
C) The pledge becomes a trust and no compensation is due.
D) The lender must return the barley and take the pledge.

Key Takeaway
A compromise on a debt secured by a pledge does not shift the pledge's security; it remains guaranteed by the original debt due to the principle of constructive receipt.

Gift of Capital After Compromise and Pledge Perishes
Ruling on obligation if capital is gifted after compromise and pledge perishes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a gift of capital follows a compromise in a salam contract and the pledge perishes, and explain why the iqalah remains binding.

Core Idea
A gift does not revoke the iqalah; the pledgee still owes the muslam fih.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in a salam contract where, after a compromise (sulh) on the capital, the seller gifts the capital back to the buyer. The question then arises: does the gift invalidate the iqalah? The Hanafi ruling clarifies that the iqalah remains valid; therefore, if the previously pledged slave perishes, the buyer must still deliver the muslam fih. The rationale is that the iqalah in salam is a binding transaction that does not admit invalidation, and the pledge's destruction is deemed as receipt of the commodity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sequential events
First, a compromise (sulh) occurs on the capital in a salam contract. Second, the seller gifts the capital to the buyer. Third, the pledged slave perishes.
The order matters: the gift comes after the compromise.
2
State the resulting obligation
The buyer must deliver food (or its equivalent) to the seller, because the pledge's destruction is treated as receipt of the muslam fih.
The obligation is to pay a similar food, not to return the capital.
3
Analyze the legal reason (ta'lil)
The iqalah in salam is binding and does not admit invalidation. The gift does not nullify it; the pledge remains as if it were already delivered.
This is based on the principle that iqalah is like a sale rescission that cannot be undone by a later act.
4
Note the cross-reference
The author mentions that the same rule applies in the chapter on sarf (currency exchange), indicating a broader principle.
Sarf also involves immediate exchange, similar to salam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وهب له رأس المال بعد الصلح ثم هلك العبد عليه طعام مثله لأن الإقالة لم تبطل بهبة رأس المال لأن الإقالة في السلم لا تقبل البطلان ففي الرهن مضموناً في المسلم فيه، وذكر مسألته في الصرف.
And if he gave him the capital as a gift after the compromise, and then the slave perished, he owes food similar to it, because the iqalah is not invalidated by the gift of capital, for iqalah in salam does not admit invalidation; thus in the pledge it is guaranteed in the muslam fih. And he mentioned its like in sarf (exchange).
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This is a continuation of the previous scenario. After the compromise (sulh) on the capital, if the seller/muslam ilayh then gives the capital as a gift to the buyer, the iqalah (compromise) remains valid. The iqalah in salam is not voided by gift. Therefore, if the pledge (slave) perishes later, the buyer (pledgee) still owes the food (muslam fih) to the seller. The pledge's destruction is treated as receipt of the food, so the buyer must pay the food. The author notes this rule is also mentioned in the chapter on sarf (currency exchange).

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): A gift; voluntary transfer of ownership without consideration.
إقالة (iqalah): A compromise or rescission of a contract by mutual agreement.
سلم (salam): A forward sale where payment is made in advance for deferred delivery of a commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The buyer owes the seller food like the original muslam fih.
C) The buyer keeps the pledge and owes nothing.
D) The seller must return the capital and the buyer keeps the pledge.

Key Takeaway
The iqalah in salam is binding and not invalidated by a subsequent gift; the pledgee's obligation to deliver the muslam fih remains.

Pledge in Invalid Sarf and the Effect of Separation
Ruling on pledge in invalid sarf due to separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a pledge in a corrupt sarf contract when separation occurs before full payment, and apply the rule of constructive possession.

Core Idea
In sarf, separation before full possession invalidates the contract; a pledge lost in the session can cure the defect via constructive receipt.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq illustrates a complex scenario in currency exchange (sarf) combined with a pledge (rahn). It demonstrates how the Hanafi requirement of immediate possession (taqabud) interacts with the pledge's guarantee, and how the timing of the pledge's loss affects the contract's validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction
The buyer purchases 1000 dirhams for 100 dinars, takes possession of the dirhams, then pledges an item worth 100 dinars for the unpaid dinars. Note the two independent contracts: sarf and rahn.
The pledge is given after partial possession of the price.
2
Analyse the effect of separation
The parties separate before the buyer pays the dinars. In sarf, both counter-values must be possessed in the session. Separation before full possession renders the sarf contract fasid (corrupt).
The dirhams are already possessed, but the dinars are not yet received.
3
Determine the status of the pledge
The pledge is held for the debt (dinars) and its substitute (dirhams), because the dirhams become a substitute for the dinars due to the corrupt contract. Thus the pledge is detained for the dinars but guaranteed by the dirhams.
This dual role is key to the later rulings on loss.
4
Apply the ruling for pledge loss after separation
If the pledge perishes after separation, the buyer (pledgee) is deemed to have received the dinars constructively, but the sarf is still invalid. Therefore, the owner of the pledge can claim 100 dinars from the buyer, and the buyer can reclaim the 1000 dirhams.
The pledge's loss is treated as actual receipt for the dinars, but the contract's invalidity persists.
5
Contrast the scenario if the pledge is lost before separation
If the parties do not separate and the pledge perishes, the constructive receipt of the dinars occurs within the session, making the sarf valid. The buyer is considered to have actually received the dinars.
This distinction hinges on timing – before or after separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية اشترى ألف درهم بمائة دينار وقبض الألف فقبض بالمائة الدينار رهناً يساويها ثم تفرقا فسد البيع لأن الافتراق قبل قبض الدنانير فصارت الدراهم مقبوضة في يد مشتريها بحكم صرف فاسد وليس له أخذ الرهن حتى يرد الألف، فإن هلك الرهن عنده رجع صاحبه عليه بمائة دينار والمرتهن بالألف لأن الدراهم بدل عن الدنانير والرهن بالشيء يكون رهناً به وببدله فيكون محبوساً بالدنانير مضموناً بالدراهم، فإذا هلك الرهن صار مستوفياً للدنانير بحكم صرف فاسد فكان على المرتهن رد الدنانير وعلى الراهن رد الدراهم، فإن لم يفترقا حتى ضاع الرهن فهو بالمائة الدينار لأنه صار مستوفياً للدنانير في المجلس حكماً بهلاك الرهن فيصير كما لو استوفى حقيقة فكان الصرف جائزاً.
Second: He bought one thousand dirhams with one hundred dinars, and he took possession of the thousand, then he took a pledge worth one hundred dinars for the dinars. Then they separated. The sale becomes void because separation occurred before possession of the dinars, so the dirhams become possessed in the buyer's hand by a corrupt sarf (currency exchange) ruling. He may not take the pledge until he returns the thousand. If the pledge perishes in his hand, its owner can claim one hundred dinars from him, and the pledgee (the buyer) can claim the thousand, because the dirhams are a substitute for the dinars, and a pledge for a thing is a pledge for it and for its substitute; so it is detained for the dinars and guaranteed by the dirhams. So when the pledge perishes, he becomes in receipt of the dinars by the ruling of a corrupt sarf; so the pledgee must return the dinars and the pledgor must return the dirhams. If they do not separate until the pledge is lost, then it is for one hundred dinars, because he becomes in receipt of the dinars in the session constructively by the perishing of the pledge, so it is as if he actually received them, and thus the sarf contract becomes valid.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage deals with a currency exchange (sarf) transaction. Someone buys 1000 dirhams with 100 dinars. He takes the dirhams, then gives a pledge equal to 100 dinars for the dinars he still owes. If they separate before he pays the dinars, the sale is invalid (fasid) because possession in sarf must be immediate. The dirhams are already possessed by a corrupt contract. He cannot take the pledge until returning the dirhams. If the pledge perishes, the other party (the creditor) can claim 100 dinars from him (the buyer) because the pledge is deemed receipt of the dinars, and the buyer can claim the 1000 dirhams back. So both must return what they took. However, if they do NOT separate (i.e., remain in the session), and the pledge is lost, then the constructive receipt of the dinars occurs within the session, making the sarf contract valid (because possession of the dinars is deemed to have occurred). Key: the pledge is considered a substitute for the debt.

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): Currency exchange; sale of money for money; requires immediate possession (taqabud) in the session.
رهن (rahn): Pledge; a contract where property is given as security for a debt.
افتراق (iftiraq): Separation of the parties from the contract session.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession; physical or constructive receipt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the sarf scenario, if the parties separate before the dinars are paid, and the pledge perishes after separation, what is the mutual obligation?
A) The buyer keeps the dirhams and owes the value of the pledge.
B) The pledgee must return the dinars and the pledgor must return the dirhams.
C) The contract becomes valid and the buyer must pay the dinars.
D) The seller must deliver the dinars and the buyer returns the dirhams.

Key Takeaway
In sarf, a pledge does not satisfy the condition of immediate possession; separation before payment invalidates the contract, but loss of the pledge in the session can cure it via constructive receipt.

Guarantee of Pledge in Compromise on a Deposit Claim
Ruling on guarantee of pledge after compromise on deposit claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the guarantee of a pledge taken under a compromise on a deposit claim, and explain the tarjih based on Abu Yusuf's retraction.

Core Idea
A pledge taken under a compromise remains guaranteed even if the deposit claim was false, because the compromise creates a valid surface obligation.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the status of a pledge (rahn) taken through a compromise (sulh) when the underlying claim was about a deposit (wadi'ah). The dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hinges on whether the depositary's statement of return invalidates the compromise. Understanding this ikhtilaf and its resolution clarifies the Hanafi position on the reliability of compromises in resolving disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the first scenario involves a general debt claim and the second involves a deposit claim where the depositary claims return. The second scenario is the focus of the dispute.
2
Understand the deposit dispute
After a compromise and a pledge, both parties later agree the deposit was returned. Abu Yusuf initially held the pledge is not guaranteed (amanah) because the depositary's statement of return establishes release, making the compromise void. Muhammad held the compromise is valid, so the pledge is guaranteed.
3
Analyze the two positions
Abu Yusuf's first view: bara'ah (release) is established by the depositary's statement, so the compromise is void and the pledge is a trust (amanah). Muhammad's view: the compromise is a valid contract independent of the original claim, creating a new obligation; the pledge is therefore guaranteed (mardud).
The text states that Abu Yusuf retracted this view to adopt Muhammad's position, and 'وهو الصحيح' (it is the correct view).
4
Apply the tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the pledge is guaranteed. If the pledge perishes, the pledgor must compensate the pledgee for the value, because the compromise created a debt in appearance.
This ruling applies even if both parties later agree no debt existed originally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ادعى على آخر فأنكره فصالحه على خمسمائة فأعطاه به رهناً وهلك الرهن ثم اتفقا على أن لا دين يجبره على قضاء خمسمائة درهم للمرتهن لأنه لو أخذ الرهن بدين ثابت من حيث الظاهر بدليل أن القاضي بعد الصلح قبل التصادق أن لا دين يجبره على قضاء والرهن بدین ثابت ظاهر مضمون على المرتهن لأن الرهن المقبوض بجهة القرض مضمون مع أن الدين غير ثابت فالرهن بدین ثابت ظاهراً ولا يكون مضموناً لأن الرهن يملك في حق ملك اليد والحبس بإزاء ما عليه من الدين والراهن لم يرض بقليله مجاناً بل رهن بشرط العوض وهو سقوط الدين بإزائه ولو كانت الدعوى في وديعة فقال المودع رددتها ثم اصطلحاً على خمسمائة وأخذ بها رهناً فهلك ثم تصادقا أنه ردها فالرهن غير مضمون عند أبي يوسف وهي كالمسألة التي قبلها . ولو ادعى صاحب الوديعة استهلاكاً ولم يدع المودع شيئاً حتى صالحه ثم رهنه فهلك الرهن ثم اتفقا على الهلاك هلك الرهن مضموناً بلا خلاف. وذكر محمد رحمه رجوع أبي يوسف رحمه الله عن هذا القول إلى قول محمد رحمهما الله تعالى وهو الصحيح، وهذا بناء على أن هذا الصلح لا يجوز في قوله أولاً وفي قوله الآخر يجوز وهو قول محمد. وجه قوله الأول أن البراءة عن الضمان تثبت بقول المودع كان الصلح باطلاً، ووجه قوله الآخر خمسمائة درهم
And if someone claimed against another, and he denied, then he compromised with him for five hundred, and gave him a pledge for it, and the pledge perished; then they agreed that there was no debt, he compels him to pay five hundred dirhams to the pledgee because he took the pledge for a debt established in appearance; evidence: the qadi after the compromise before the agreement that there is no debt compels him to pay. And the pledge for a debt established in appearance is guaranteed on the pledgee because a pledge possessed by way of loan is guaranteed even though the debt is not fixed. So a pledge for a debt apparently fixed is not without guarantee, because the pledge is owned in terms of possession and detention in exchange for what is due from him, and the pledgor did not give his property for free but pledged with condition of compensation, i.e., the dropping of the debt in return. And if the claim was about a deposit (wadi'ah) and the depositary said 'I returned it', then they compromised for five hundred and he took a pledge for it, and it perished, then they agreed that he had returned it: the pledge is not guaranteed according to Abu Yusuf, and it is like the previous issue. And if the owner of the deposit claimed destruction, and the depositary did not claim anything until he compromised with him and then gave him a pledge, and the pledge perished, then they agreed on the destruction: the pledge perishes as guaranteed without disagreement. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned the retraction of Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) from this statement to the statement of Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them both), and that is the correct view. This is based on the fact that this compromise is not valid in his first statement, and in his later statement it is valid, and that is the statement of Muhammad. The basis of his first statement: the release from guarantee is established by the depositary's statement, so the compromise was void. The basis of his other statement: five hundred dirhams.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where someone makes a false claim, the defendant denies, but they compromise for 500 and the claimant takes a pledge. Later they agree there was no debt. The ruling: the pledge remains guaranteed because the compromise created a debt in appearance (zahir). The qadi would enforce payment based on the compromise. The pledgor is liable for the pledge. Then a sub-case: if the original claim was about a deposit (wadi'ah) and the depositary claimed he returned it, then they compromise for 500 and take a pledge; later they agree the depositary had returned it. According to Abu Yusuf, the pledge is not guaranteed (amanah). The text then mentions that Imam Muhammad reported that Abu Yusuf retracted this view and adopted Muhammad's opinion, which is correct. The dispute hinges on whether such a compromise on a deposit claim is valid. In the first view, it is invalid because the depositary's statement of return gives him release (bara'ah), so the compromise is void. In the second view, the compromise is valid, so the pledge is guaranteed. The text ends abruptly with 'خمسمائة درهم'.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A compromise agreement to settle a dispute; in Hanafi law, it is a contract that can create a new obligation.
وديعة (wadi'ah): Deposit; a trust where the depositary holds property for safekeeping.
رهن (rahn): Pledge; property given as security for a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a pledge taken under a compromise remains guaranteed even if the underlying claim is later admitted false, because the compromise creates a new surface-level obligation.

Ruling on Pledge When Debt Is Settled with Different Goods
Explains ruling when a pledge is given for wheat and then settled for barley

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a pledge when the debt is settled with different goods, and apply the principle of constructive satisfaction (istifa' hukmi) to determine the guarantee status of the pledge.

Core Idea
Settlement with different goods transfers the pledge's guarantee to the new debt through constructive satisfaction.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced scenario in the law of pledge (rahn): what happens when a lender of wheat takes a pledge and later agrees to settle the debt for barley. The Hanafi school rules that the pledge remains security for the barley, but if destroyed, compensation is tied to the original wheat debt. The key legal concept is constructive satisfaction (al-istifa' al-hukmi), which treats the settlement as effective as actual payment for the purpose of transferring the pledge's guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the transaction and what is the legal status of the pledge? The act is settling a debt of wheat for barley when a pledge exists. The ruling: the pledge becomes security for the barley, but if destroyed, it is guaranteed by the original wheat.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The author uses qiyas (analogy) to actual payment and rescission in salam. Constructive satisfaction is likened to actual satisfaction.
Dalil type: qiyas khafi
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal reasoning)
Constructive satisfaction (istifa' hukmi) is analogous to actual satisfaction; thus, just as actual payment discharges the debt and transfers the pledge's guarantee, constructive satisfaction does the same.
4
Note the scope and exceptions
A pledge can be merely detained (mahbus) without being guaranteed (e.g., its increases). But here, the pledge becomes guaranteed due to constructive satisfaction.
5
Distinguish the loan case
If after actual receipt of wheat the parties settle for barley, the settlement is invalid. But after constructive satisfaction (i.e., before actual receipt), it is valid. This highlights the difference between actual and constructive satisfaction.
Adjacent ruling

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقرض رجلاً كر حنطة وارتهن منه ثوباً قيمته أو صالحه من عليه الحنطة على كر شعير بعينه ويصير الثوب رهناً بالشعير، فإذا هلك يهلك مضموناً بالحنطة لأنه برى عن الحنطة فصار كما لو برئ بالإيفاء ويجوز أن يكون الشيء رهناً ولا يكون مضموناً كزوائد الرهن يكون محبوساً ولا يكون مضموناً، وذلك لأن الرهن استيفاء حكمي والاستيفاء الحكمي لا يربو على الاستيفاء الحقيقي. ولو استوفى المسلم فيه حقيقة ثم تقايلا السلم صحت الإقالة ويرد عليه طعاماً مثله ويأخذ رأس ماله فكذا إذا اصطلحا بعد الاستيفاء الحكمي. وفي مسألة القرض لو صالحه على الشعير بعدما استوفى الحنطة حقيقة لم يجز الصلح لأنه لو صالحه على دين وليس عليه ذلك الدين يصح أصلاً فكذا إذا اصطلحا بعد الاستيفاء الحكمي. ولو وهب له رأس المال بعد الصلح ثم هلك العبد عليه طعام مثله لأن الإقالة لم تبطل بهبة رأس المال لأن الإقالة في السلم لا تقبل البطلان ففي الرهن مضموناً في المسلم فيه، وذكر مسألته في الصرف.
A man lent a kurr of wheat and took a garment as a pledge worth it, or he settled with the one who owed the wheat for a specific kurr of barley, and the garment becomes a pledge for the barley. If it is destroyed, it is destroyed as guaranteed by the wheat because he has become free from the wheat, so it is as if he became free by payment. And it is permissible that a thing be a pledge and not be guaranteed, like the increases of the pledge are detained and not guaranteed. That is because the pledge is constructive satisfaction, and constructive satisfaction does not exceed actual satisfaction. And if one received the salam goods actually, then they rescinded the salam, the rescission is valid, and he returns similar food and takes his capital; so likewise if they settled after constructive satisfaction. And in the case of a loan, if he settled with him for barley after he had actually received the wheat, the settlement is not valid because if he settles for a debt that is not owed, it is valid originally; so likewise if they settled after constructive satisfaction. And if he gifted him the capital after the settlement, then the slave is destroyed; he owes similar food because the rescission is not nullified by the gift of the capital, because rescission in salam does not accept nullification. So in the pledge, it is guaranteed in the salam goods, and his case is mentioned in the chapter on currency exchange.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, discussing the case where a lender of wheat takes a pledge and later settles for a specific quantity of barley. The key point is that the pledge becomes security for the new debt (barley) because the original debt is discharged. However, if the pledge is destroyed, it is guaranteed by the original debt (wheat) because the discharge is constructive, not actual. The author uses qiyas (analogy) to explain that constructive satisfaction (al-istifa' al-hukmi) does not exceed actual satisfaction. He then compares to the case of rescission (iqala) in salam, where similar rules apply. The passage also clarifies that a pledge can be merely detained (mahbus) without being guaranteed (mamun), as in the case of its increases. Finally, a sub-ruling: if after the settlement the lender gifts the capital (the wheat) to the debtor, the rescission remains valid and the pledge remains guaranteed.

Key Terms
استيفاء حكمي (istifa' hukmi): Constructive satisfaction: when a debt is considered satisfied through a legal transaction (like settlement) without actual payment.
مضمون (mamun): Guaranteed: the pledge is subject to liability; if destroyed, the creditor is entitled to compensation from the debtor or the pledge value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The barley
B) The wheat
C) The value of the pledge
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
A pledge taken for a debt remains guaranteed by the original debt even after the debt is settled with different goods, because constructive satisfaction (istifa' hukmi) is equivalent to actual payment in Hanafi law.

Invalid Currency Sale due to Separation before Possession
Ruling on a specific case of sarf with rahn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a currency exchange where the parties separate before full possession, and explain the effect of a pledge being destroyed in the session.

Core Idea
In sarf, separation before possession makes the contract fasid, but if the pledge is destroyed in the session, constructive possession occurs and the sale becomes valid.

This case illustrates a critical condition in Hanafi currency exchange (sarf): immediate possession (qabd) in the session. The text walks through a scenario where a buyer gives a pledge instead of the dinars, then separates – leading to a fasid sale. It then explores how the destruction of the pledge can, under certain conditions, convert the sale into a valid one through constructive possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and the condition violated
Determine the act (sarf) and the missing essential condition – possession of one countervalue in the session. Note that the dirhams are taken, but the dinars are not; only a pledge is given. Separation before receiving the dinars triggers the violation.
The condition of qabd in the session is unique to sarf in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Determine the effect of separation
Separation (iftiraq) before possession of both countervalues makes the contract fasid (invalid but not void). The dirhams become held under a fasid contract, and the buyer may not take the pledge until returning the dirhams.
Fasid contracts are not completely void; they can sometimes be rectified.
3
Analyze the status of the pledge
The pledge is detained for the dinars but guaranteed for the dirhams because the dirhams are a substitute for the dinars. If the pledge is destroyed, the buyer is considered to have constructively received the dinars (qabd hukmi) under a fasid contract.
The pledge's dual role: secured for the dinars and guaranteed for the dirhams.
4
Consider the consequence of pledge destruction in vs. after the session
If the pledge is destroyed after separation, the contract remains fasid; each party must return what they owe. If destroyed before separation, constructive possession of the dinars occurs in the session, making the sale valid.
The timing of destruction relative to separation is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية اشترى ألف درهم بمائة دينار وقبض الألف فقبض بالمائة الدينار رهناً يساويها ثم تفرقا فسد البيع لأن الافتراق قبل قبض الدنانير فصارت الدراهم مقبوضة في يد مشتريها بحكم صرف فاسد وليس له أخذ الرهن حتى يرد الألف، فإن هلك الرهن عنده رجع صاحبه عليه بمائة دينار والمرتهن بالألف لأن الدراهم بدل عن الدنانير والرهن بالشيء يكون رهناً به وببدله فيكون محبوساً بالدنانير مضموناً بالدراهم، فإذا هلك الرهن صار مستوفياً للدنانير بحكم صرف فاسد فكان على المرتهن رد الدنانير وعلى الراهن رد الدراهم، فإن لم يفترقا حتى ضاع الرهن فهو بالمائة الدينار لأنه صار مستوفياً للدنانير في المجلس حكماً بهلاك الرهن فيصير كما لو استوفى حقيقة فكان الصرف جائزاً.
The second case: He bought one thousand dirhams for one hundred dinars and he received the thousand, so he received a pledge equal to one hundred dinars for the dinars. Then they separated, so the sale is invalid because separation occurred before receiving the dinars. Thus the dirhams become received in the hand of their buyer under a fasid currency contract, and he may not take the pledge until he returns the thousand. If the pledge is destroyed in his possession, his companion (the seller) has recourse against him for one hundred dinars, and the mortgagee (the buyer) has recourse for the thousand, because the dirhams are in place of the dinars, and the pledge for a thing becomes a pledge for it and its substitute. So it is detained for the dinars and guaranteed for the dirhams. If the pledge is destroyed, he becomes having received the dinars under a fasid currency contract, so the mortgagee must return the dinars and the pledgor must return the dirhams. If they have not separated until the pledge is lost, then it is (secured) by the one hundred dinars, because he has constructively received the dinars in the session due to the destruction of the pledge, so it becomes as if he had actually received them, and the currency sale is valid.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement describing a case in currency exchange (sarf). A buyer purchases 1000 dirhams for 100 dinars. He takes the dirhams and gives a pledge worth 100 dinars for the dinars. Before the dinars are actually received, the parties separate. This makes the sale invalid (fasid) because in sarf, possession must be in the session. The dirhams are then held under a fasid contract. The pledge is detained for the dinars but guaranteed for the dirhams. If the pledge is destroyed, the buyer is considered to have constructively received the dinars, so each party must return what they owe. If they separate after the pledge is destroyed without separation, the sale becomes valid because constructive receipt occurred in the session.

Key Terms
صرف فاسد (sarf fasid): An invalid currency exchange contract due to failure of an essential condition (like possession in the session).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the buyer did not intend to pay
B) Because separation occurred before receiving the dinars
C) Because the pledge was destroyed
D) Because the buyer took the dirhams first

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sarf, immediate possession in the session is a condition; separation before full possession makes the contract fasid, but constructive possession via destruction of the pledge in the session can validate it.

Pledge Under Disputed Debt: No Guarantee When Debt Nonexistent
Explains that if a disputed debt is settled with a pledge and later both agree there was no debt, the pledge is not guaranteed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a pledge taken under a disputed debt that is later admitted to be nonexistent, and explain the ta'lil (legal reasoning) for the pledge not being guaranteed.

Core Idea
A pledge taken for an outwardly established debt does not become guaranteed if the debt never truly existed.

This lesson examines a specific case in Hanafi fiqh: a debt is claimed, denied, then settled with a pledge. Later, both parties admit there was no debt. What happens to the pledge? The text explains that the pledge is not guaranteed, even though it was taken for a debt that appeared established. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between reality and appearance in legal transactions.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the scenario
Determine the facts: a claim, denial, settlement with a pledge, later mutual admission that no debt existed.
2
2. Recognize the apparent debt
The settlement created a 'dayn thabit zahir' (debt established in appearance). Understand that this is not actual, but legally treated as real for certain purposes.
3
3. Determine the initial consequence
The judge would initially compel payment of the settlement amount because of the apparent debt, treating the pledge as guaranteed.
4
4. Apply the later admission
Once both admit no debt existed, the apparent debt disappears retroactively. The pledge loses its guarantee because the condition for guarantee (real debt) is absent.
5
5. Derive the final ruling
The pledge is valid (sahih) but not guaranteed (ghayr mamun). The pledgor's consent was conditional on discharging a debt; since no debt existed, the pledge is held without compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ادعى على آخر فأنكره فصالحه على خمسمائة فأعطاه به رهناً وهلك الرهن ثم اتفقا على أن لا دين يجبره على قضاء خمسمائة درهم للمرتهن لأنه لو أخذ الرهن بدين ثابت من حيث الظاهر بدليل أن القاضي بعد الصلح قبل التصادق أن لا دين يجبره على قضاء والرهن بدین ثابت ظاهر مضمون على المرتهن لأن الرهن المقبوض بجهة القرض مضمون مع أن الدين غير ثابت فالرهن بدین ثابت ظاهراً ولا يكون مضموناً لأن الرهن يملك في حق ملك اليد والحبس بإزاء ما عليه من الدين والراهن لم يرض بقليله مجاناً بل رهن بشرط العوض وهو سقوط الدين بإزائه
And if one person claimed against another and he denied, then he settled with him for five hundred and gave him a pledge for it, and the pledge was destroyed, then they both agreed that there is no debt; (the judge) compels him to pay five hundred dirhams to the mortgagee because he took the pledge for a debt established in terms of appearance, as evidenced by the fact that the judge, after the settlement, before the mutual admission of no debt, compels payment. And the pledge for a debt that is established in appearance is guaranteed upon the mortgagee because the pledge taken by way of loan is guaranteed even though the debt is not established. So the pledge for a debt that is established externally is not guaranteed because the pledge is owned in terms of possession and detention in exchange for the debt owed, and the pledgor did not consent to giving it for free; rather, he pledged with the condition of compensation, which is the discharge of the debt in exchange for it.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining the case where a person claims a debt, the defendant denies, but they settle for a sum and a pledge is given. Later both admit there never was a debt. The ruling is that the pledge is not guaranteed (mamun) even though it was taken for a debt that appeared established (by the settlement). The reason is that the pledge is held as security, but since the debt never existed, the pledgor did not truly owe anything. The judge originally compels payment because of the apparent debt (after settlement), but if they later agree there was no debt, the pledge becomes non-guaranteed. The text uses legal reasoning (ta'lil) to explain that the pledge is not guaranteed because the pledgor's consent was conditional on discharging a debt, and if there is no debt, the condition fails.

Key Terms
دين ثابت ظاهر (dayn thabit zahir): A debt that is established in appearance (outwardly) through a settlement, though not in reality.
مضمون (mamun): Guaranteed; subject to compensation if lost or destroyed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a defendant denies a debt, then settles with a pledge, and later both agree there was no debt, is the pledge guaranteed?
A) Yes, because the settlement established the debt
B) No, because the debt was never real
C) Yes, because the pledge was taken with consent
D) No, because the judge compels payment

Key Takeaway
A pledge taken under a settlement does not become guaranteed if the underlying debt is later discovered to be nonexistent; the pledge is only valid as a security but not subject to compensation.

Dispute on Pledge in Deposit Case with Settlement
Intra-Hanafi dispute with retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding a pledge in a deposit settlement case and apply the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling.

Core Idea
A pledge taken after settlement remains guaranteed even if return is later admitted.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a specific dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The case involves a depositary who claims he returned the deposit, then settles with the depositor, giving a pledge that is destroyed, and later both admit the return. The question is whether the pledge is guaranteed. The correct view is that it is guaranteed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the facts of the case
The claim is about a deposit (wadi'ah). The depositary (mudi') says 'I returned it'. They settle for 500 and the depositary gives a pledge (rahn). The pledge is destroyed. Later they mutually admit that the deposit was indeed returned.
This is the first scenario; a second scenario where the depositary admits misappropriation is also mentioned but not disputed.
2
State Abu Yusuf's initial view and its reasoning
Abu Yusuf initially held that the pledge is not guaranteed (ghayr madmun). His reasoning: the depositary's claim of return, if later admitted, establishes that he was discharged from liability (bara'a) from the moment of the claim. Thus the settlement was invalid (batil) because there was no actual debt, and the pledge given under an invalid settlement is not guaranteed.
This is his qawl al-awwal.
3
State Muhammad's view and its reasoning
Muhammad held that the pledge is guaranteed (madmun). His reasoning: at the time of the settlement, the depositary's claim of return was not yet proven; the settlement created an apparent debt. The subsequent admission does not retroactively invalidate the settlement. Hence the pledge is enforceable.
Muhammad also reports that Abu Yusuf retracted to this view.
4
Determine the relied-upon position and the practical consequence
The correct (sahih) position is Muhammad's: the pledge is guaranteed. The practical consequence: if the pledge is destroyed, the creditor can claim its value from the depositary, even if the deposit return is later admitted.
This is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانت الدعوى في وديعة فقال المودع رددتها ثم اصطلحاً على خمسمائة وأخذ بها رهناً فهلك ثم تصادقا أنه ردها فالرهن غير مضمون عند أبي يوسف وهي كالمسألة التي قبلها . ولو ادعى صاحب الوديعة استهلاكاً ولم يدع المودع شيئاً حتى صالحه ثم رهنه فهلك الرهن ثم اتفقا على الهلاك هلك الرهن مضموناً بلا خلاف. وذكر محمد رحمه رجوع أبي يوسف رحمه الله عن هذا القول إلى قول محمد رحمهما الله تعالى وهو الصحيح، وهذا بناء على أن هذا الصلح لا يجوز في قوله أولاً وفي قوله الآخر يجوز وهو قول محمد. وجه قوله الأول أن البراءة عن الضمان تثبت بقول المودع كان الصلح باطلاً، ووجه قوله الآخر مذكور فيه وقوله مضمون.
And if the claim is concerning a deposit, and the depositary said 'I returned it', then they settled for five hundred and he took a pledge for it, and it was destroyed, then they mutually admitted that he had returned it, then the pledge is not guaranteed according to Abu Yusuf, and it is like the preceding case. And if the owner of the deposit claimed misappropriation, and the depositary did not claim anything, until he settled with him, then he gave a pledge, then the pledge was destroyed, then they agreed on the destruction, the pledge is destroyed as guaranteed without dispute. And Muhammad related that Abu Yusuf retracted from this view to the view of Muhammad, and it is the correct one. This is based on the fact that this settlement is not permissible in his first view, and in his other view it is permissible, which is the view of Muhammad. The argument for his first view is that the discharge from liability is established by the statement of the depositary, so the settlement was invalid. The argument for his other view is mentioned in it, and his statement 'guaranteed'.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The first case: after a settlement on a returned deposit, the pledge is not guaranteed according to Abu Yusuf, similar to the previous case. The second case: if the depositary admits misappropriation (or does not deny), then settles and gives a pledge, the pledge is guaranteed without dispute. The key dispute is on the first case: Abu Yusuf initially said the pledge is not guaranteed because the depositary's claim of return, if admitted later, voids the settlement. But later he retracted to Muhammad's view that the settlement is valid and the pledge is guaranteed. The correct position (sahih) is that the pledge is guaranteed because the settlement is valid. The reasoning: the depositary's initial claim of return, if taken as true, would mean the debt is discharged, so the settlement is invalid; but if the settlement is accepted as valid, then the pledge is guaranteed.

Key Terms
البراءة عن الضمان (al-bara'a 'an al-daman): Discharge from liability: the depositary is freed from responsibility for the deposit if he proves return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the deposit case where the depositary claims return, then they settle with a pledge, and later admit return, what is the correct ruling on the pledge?
A) Not guaranteed (Abu Yusuf's initial view)
B) Guaranteed (Muhammad's view)
C) Void (batil)
D) Dependent on the judge's discretion

Key Takeaway
The settlement creates an outward debt that makes the pledge guaranteed, even if the original debt is later shown not to exist.
