The Order of Producing the Pledge and Paying the Debt in Hanafi Rahn
Rulings on ordering the mortgagee to produce the pledge and the mortgagor to pay, with special scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the mortgagee must produce the pledge before the debtor pays, and apply it to variations such as different city, heavy pledge, sale of pledge, and dispute with Zufar.

Core Idea
Produce the pledge first, then pay the debt — this order protects both parties from double satisfaction.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the procedural order in rahn: when the mortgagee demands his debt, he must first bring the pledge to confirm it exists. This mirrors the order in a sale where the seller brings the goods. Several scenarios are then examined, from differing cities to disputes with third parties, illustrating how the core rule adapts to protect both parties and prevent injustice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its analogy
Locate the statement that the mortgagee is ordered to produce the pledge and the pledgor to pay the debt. Note the analogy to sale: the seller brings the goods, then the buyer pays. Understand the rationale: proving existence and avoiding double satisfaction.
The matn says 'ويؤمر المرتهن بإحضار رهنه والراهن بإداء دينه'.
2
Analyze the exception for different city
Read the condition: if the pledge is in a different city, distinguish between light and heavy pledges. For light pledges, the same rule applies; for heavy ones, only takhliyah (release) is required, not transport.
Takhliyah means releasing possession without physical movement.
3
Examine the scenario after sale of the pledge
If the pledge is sold with the pledgor's permission, the buyer is not required to bring it. After the seller receives the price, he must produce the price as a substitute because it stands in place of the pledge.
The ruling that 'البدل مقام المبدل' (the substitute takes the place of the substituted).
4
Understand the third-party deposit scenarios
When the pledge is with a third party (adl), the mortgagee is not required to bring it because he never had possession. The pledgor must pay directly. If the third party denies the pledge, the mortgagee loses recourse until he proves it.
Adl is a trusted depositary, not the mortgagee's agent.
5
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note the final ruling that a pledge for a specific dower in marriage is invalid according to the majority, contrary to Zufar. Understand the proof and the thamarah al-khilaf.
Zufar holds that a specific obligation can be secured; the majority requires a debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ويؤمر المرتهن بإحضار رهنه والراهن بإداء دينه أو لا أي إذا طلب المرتهن دينه يؤمر بإحضار الرهن أولاً ليعلم أنه باق ولأنه قبض الرهن قبل الاستيفاء، ولا يجوز أن يقبض ما له مع قيام يد الاستيفاء لأنه يؤدي إلى تكرار الاستيفاء على اعتبار الهلاك في يد المرتهن وهو يحتمل ولو قال بإحضار رهنه لو في يده لكان أولى ليخرج ما إذا كان في يد عدل فإنه لا يؤمر بإحضاره كما سنبين، وإذا أحضر المرتهن الرهن أمر الراهن بتسليم الدين أولاً وهو المراد بقوله والراهن بإداء دينه أولاً» ليتعين حق المرتهن في الدين كما تعين حق الراهن في حق الرهن تحقيقاً للتسوية بينهما كما في تسليم المبيع والثمن يحضر البائع المبيع ثم يسلم المشتري الثمن الأول لما ذكرنا. وإن طالبه بالدين في غير البلد الذي وقع العقد فيه فإن كان الرهن لا حمل له ولا مؤنة فكذلك الجواب لأن الأماكن كلها في حقه كبقعة واحدة في حق التسليم ولهذا لا يشترط فيه بيان مكان الإيفاء فيه في باب السلم بالإجماعه وإن كان له حمل ومؤنة فيستوفي دينه ولا يكلف إحضار الرهن لأن الواجب عليه التسليم بالتخلية دون النقل لأنه يتضرر به زيادة ضرر لم تلزمه في العقد. ولو باع الرهن لا يكلف المشتري إحضار الرهن لأنه لا قدرة له عليه لأن بيعه بأمر الراهن صحيح وصار الرهن ديناً فصار كأنه رهنه الراهن وهو دين. ولو قبض الثمن يكلف إحضاره لقيام البدل مقام المبدل والذي يقبض الثمن هو البائع، مرتهناً كان أو ترجع إليه ويكلف إحضار الرهن باستيفاء كل الدين العاقد وحقوق العقد هو عدلاً، لأنه الرهن في يد المرتهن لا يمكنه من البيع حتى يقبض الدين فإذا قضى سلم الرهن ولا ينتفع المرتهن بالرهن استخداماً وسكنى ولبساً وإجارة وإعارة ويحفظ بنفسه وزوجته يكلف باستيفاء نجم قد حل إذا ادعى الراهن هلاكه لاحتمال الهلاك بخلاف ما إذا لم يدع الراهن هلاكه لأنه لا فائدة في إحضاره مع إقراره وهذا بخلاف ما إذا قتل رجل خطأ العبد الرهن حتى قضى بالقيمة على عاقلته في ثلاث سنين حيث لا يجبر الراهن وفيما تقدم صار ديناً بفعله ولا بد من إحضار جميع القيمة لأنه يقوم مقام العين لكونها بدلاً عنها. ولو وضع الرهن على يد عدل وأذن بالإيداع ففعل ثم جاء المرتهن فطلب دينه لا يكلف إحضاره لأنه لم يؤتمن عليه حيث وضع على يد غيره فلم يكن تسليمه في قدرته وكذا لو وضعه العدل في يد من في عياله وغاب وطلب المرتهن دينه والذي في يده الرهن يقر بالوديعة من العدل ويقول لا أدري لمن هو يجبر الراهن على قضاء الدين لأن إحضار الرهن ليس على المرتهن لأنه لم يقبض، وكذا إذا غاب العدل ولا يدري أين هو لما قلنا بخلاف ما إذا جحد الذي أودعه العدل الرهن بأن قال هو مالي حيث لا يرجع المرتهن على الراهن بشيء حتى يثبت أنه رهن لأنه لما جحد فقد توى المال والتوى على المرتهن فتحقق الاستيفاء فلا يملك المطالبة به. وفي الفتاوى الغياثية ولو رهن الذمي خمراً عند مسلم كان مضموناً عليه بالدين اهـ. وفي الينابيع : لو تزوج امرأة على دراهم أو دنانير بعينها وأخذ بها رهناً لم يصح عندنا خلافاً لزفر.
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And the mortgagee is ordered to produce his pledge and the pledgor is ordered to pay his debt, else ...) meaning: When the mortgagee demands his debt, he is first ordered to bring the pledge so that it is known that it still exists, and because he took possession of the pledge before satisfaction. It is not permissible for him to take what is his while continuing in possession, because that leads to double satisfaction on the assumption of destruction in the hands of the mortgagee, which is possible. And if he said 'Bringing his pledge if it is in his hand' would be more appropriate to exclude the case where it is with a third party; then he is not ordered to bring it, as we will explain. When the mortgagee brings the pledge, the pledgor is ordered to pay the debt first – this is what is meant by his statement 'and the pledgor is ordered to pay his debt first' – so that the mortgagee's right to the debt becomes fixed just as the pledgor's right to the pledge becomes fixed, achieving equality between them, similar to the delivery of goods and price: the seller brings the goods, then the buyer pays the price first. If the mortgagor demands payment in a different city from where the contract took place: if the pledge has no load or expense, the response is the same, because for this purpose all places are like a single spot for delivery; hence it is not necessary to specify a place of payment in salam sales by consensus. If it has load and expense, he may collect his debt and is not required to bring the pledge, because his obligation is delivery by release, not transport, as that would cause him additional harm not stipulated in the contract. If he sold the pledge, the buyer is not required to bring the pledge because he has no power over it; the sale is valid by the pledgor's order, and the pledge becomes a debt, so it is as if the pledgor pledged a debt. If he receives the price, he is required to bring it because the substitute takes the place of the substituted. The one who receives the price is the seller, whether he is the mortgagee or returns to him, and he is required to bring the pledge upon satisfaction of the entire debt. The contract rights are from the contracting party, and it is fair, because the pledge in the hands of the mortgagee does not enable him to sell until he receives the debt. When he pays, he surrenders the pledge. The mortgagee does not benefit from the pledge by using it, residing in it, wearing it, leasing it, or lending it; he preserves it himself and with his wife. He is required to produce it upon the falling due of a payment if the pledgor claims its destruction, because of the possibility of destruction, unlike if the pledgor does not claim destruction, because there is no benefit in bringing it with his acknowledgment. This is unlike if a man accidentally kills the pledged slave, where the value is imposed on his 'aqlah in three years; in that case the pledgor is not compelled. In the preceding, it became a debt by his act, and he must bring all the value because it stands in place of the commodity as a substitute. If the pledge is placed with a trusted third party and he is authorized to deposit, and he does so, then the mortgagee comes and demands his debt, he is not required to bring it because he was not entrusted with it; it was placed with another, so its delivery was not within his power. Similarly, if the third party placed it with someone under his care, and then disappeared, and the mortgagee demands his debt, and the one holding the pledge acknowledges the deposit from the third party and says 'I do not know whose it is', the pledgor is compelled to pay the debt because bringing the pledge is not incumbent on the mortgagee since he did not take possession. Likewise, if the third party disappears and his whereabouts are unknown, based on what we said. This differs from if the depositee denies the pledge by saying 'it is mine', in which case the mortgagee has no recourse against the pledgor until it is proven to be a pledge; because when the depositee denied, the property was lost, and the loss fell on the mortgagee, so satisfaction is achieved, and he has no right to demand it. In al-Fatawa al-Ghiyathiyyah: If a dhimmi pledges wine to a Muslim, it is guaranteed against the debtor. In al-Yanabi': If a man marries a woman for specific dirhams or dinars and takes a pledge for it, it is not valid according to us, contrary to Zufar.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling that the mortgagee must first produce the pledge before the debtor pays. This ensures the pledge still exists and avoids double satisfaction. The order of steps parallels that in sale: the seller produces the goods, then the buyer pays. Several special scenarios are then discussed:

1. If the debt is demanded in a different city: if the pledge is light, the mortgagee must bring it; if heavy, he need only release possession, not transport it.
2. If the pledge is sold by the pledgor with permission: the buyer is not required to bring it because he lacks possession; but after the seller (mortgagee) receives the price, he must bring the substitute (the price).
3. If the pledgor claims the pledge is destroyed but the mortgagee has it: if the pledgor claims destruction (without proof), the mortgagee must produce it to verify; if the pledgor does not claim destruction, production is unnecessary.
4. If the pledge is killed by someone: the value becomes a debt payable in installments, and the mortgagee must bring that value.
5. If the pledge is placed with a third party (عدل): the mortgagee is not required to bring it because he never took possession. The pledgor must pay directly.
6. If the third party denies it is a pledge: the mortgagee loses recourse against the pledgor until he proves it is a pledge.
7. Special cases: a non-Muslim pledging wine is valid; pledging for a specific dower in marriage is invalid (contrary to Zufar). Each ruling aims to balance the rights of both parties and prevent unjust enrichment.

Key Terms
تخلية (takhliyah): Delivery by release; handing over possession without physical transport.
عدل (adl): A trustworthy third party with whom the pledge is deposited.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject in a Muslim state who is allowed to practice his religion while paying jizyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because he is the mortgagee.
B) No, because he never took possession; the pledgor must pay instead.
C) Yes, only if the third party is absent.
D) No, but the third party must bring it.

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee must first prove the pledge’s existence before the debtor pays, ensuring fairness and preventing double satisfaction.

Mortgagee's Rights and Duties: Detention, Surrender, and Prohibition of Use
Rulings that the mortgagee cannot be forced to sell the pledge until paid, must surrender when paid, and cannot use the pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi rulings regarding the mortgagee's possession of a pledge and apply them to scenarios involving partial payment, destruction after payment, and unauthorized use.

Core Idea
The pledge is for security only; the mortgagee cannot use it and must surrender it upon full payment.

In Hanafi fiqh, the pledge (rahn) is a contract of security, not of benefit. The mortgagee holds the pledge solely as a guarantee for the debt. This lesson unpacks three key rulings: the mortgagee's right to retain the pledge until full payment, the obligation to return it upon payment (and the liability if it perishes in the interim), and the absolute prohibition of using the pledge without the pledgor's permission. These principles protect both parties and maintain the trust nature of the pledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ruling: mortgagee's right to detain
Read the statement that the mortgagee cannot be forced to allow sale of the pledge until he receives the debt. Understand that the purpose of rahn is permanent detention (habs da'im), not forced sale. Note that even after partial payment, the entire pledge remains detained.
This is analogous to detention of a sold item (hab al-mabi').
2
Analyze the second ruling: surrender upon payment and post-payment liability
When the debtor pays the full debt, the mortgagee must surrender the pledge. However, the pledge remains in the mortgagee's liability (madhmun) until actual delivery or formal discharge (ibra'). If the pledge perishes after payment but before delivery, the debtor recovers his payment because the destruction reveals that the mortgagee was already satisfied.
Contrast with perishing after cancellation (faskh) vs. after ibra': after ibra', the pledge ceases to be a pledge and the mortgagee is not liable.
3
Examine the third ruling: prohibition of use
The mortgagee is forbidden from using the pledge in any way (employment, residence, wearing, leasing, lending) because the pledge is only for detention, not benefit. Any use requires the pledgor's explicit permission.
This ruling is consistent across the Hanafi school and is based on the trust nature of the mortgagee's possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (فإن كان الرهن في يد المرتهن لا يمكنه من البيع حتى يقبض الدين) أي لو أراد الراهن أن يبيع الرهن لكي يقضي بثمنه الدين لا يجبر المرتهن أن يمكنه من البيع حتى يقبض الدين لأن حكم الرهن الحبس الدائم إلى أن يقضي الدين لا القضاء من ثمنه على ما بينا من قبل، فلو قضاه البعض فله أن يحبس كل الرهن حتى يستوفي البقية كما في حبس المبيع. قال رحمه الله : (فإذا قضى سلم الرهن أي إذا قضى الراهن جميع الدين سلم المرتهن الرهن إليه الزوال المانع من التسليم لوصول حق المرتهن إليه ، فلو هلك الرهن بعد قضاء الدين قبل تسليمه إلى الراهن استرد الراهن ما قضاه من الدين لأنه تبين بالهلاك أنه صار مستوفياً من وقت القبض السابق فكان الثاني استيفاء بعد استيفاء فيجب رده . وهذا لأنه بإيفاء الدين لا ينفسخ الرهن حتى يرده إلى صاحبه فيكون مضموناً على حاله بعد قضاء الدين ما لم يسلمه إلى الراهن أو يبرئه المرتهن عن الدين، وكذا لو فسخا الرهن لا ينفسخ ما دام في يده حتى كان للمرتهن أن بعد الفسخ حتى يستوفي دينه . ولو هلك بعد الفسخ يكون كما لو هلك قبله فيكون هالكاً بدينه بخلاف ما إذا هلك بعد الإبراء حيث لا يضمن استحساناً لأنه لم يبق رهناً لأن بقاءه رهناً بأمرين بالقبض والدين، فإذا مات أحدهما لم يبق رهناً وقد قدمناه مفصلاً. قال رحمه الله : (ولا ينتفع المرتهن بالرهن استخداماً وسكنى ولبساً وإجارة وإعارة) لأن الرهن يقتضي الحبس إلى أن يستوفي دينه دون الانتفاع فلا يجوز الانتفاع إلا بتسليط منه، وإن
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (If the pledge is in the hand of the mortgagee, he cannot be compelled to sell until he receives the debt) meaning if the pledgor wants to sell the pledge to pay the debt from its price, the mortgagee is not forced to enable him to sell until he receives the debt, because the ruling of the pledge is permanent detention until the debt is paid, not payment from its price, as we have explained before. If he pays part, he may detain the entire pledge until he collects the remainder, as in the detention of a sold item. He said: (When he pays, he surrenders the pledge) meaning when the pledgor pays the entire debt, the mortgagee surrenders the pledge to him, removing the impediment to delivery because the mortgagee's right has been fulfilled. If the pledge perishes after payment but before delivery to the pledgor, the pledgor recovers what he paid because the destruction reveals that the mortgagee had become satisfied from the earlier possession, so the second payment is recovery after satisfaction and thus must be returned. This is because by paying the debt the pledge is not dissolved until it is returned to its owner, so it remains in his liability until he returns it to the pledgor or the mortgagee discharges the debt. Likewise, if they both cancel the pledge, it is not dissolved as long as it is in his hand; indeed, the mortgagee has the right after cancellation to recover his debt. If it perishes after cancellation, it is as if it perished before, so it is lost against his debt, unlike if it perishes after discharge, where he is not liable by istihsan because it is no longer a pledge; the persistence of the pledge requires two things: possession and debt, and if one of them ends, it is no longer a pledge, as we have detailed. He said: (And the mortgagee does not benefit from the pledge by using it, residing in it, wearing it, leasing it, or lending it) because the pledge requires detention until the debt is recovered, not benefit; so benefit is not permissible except with his permission. And if...
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage covers three interconnected rulings about the creditor's rights over the pledge while it remains in his possession:

1. The creditor cannot be forced to allow the debtor to sell the pledge. The purpose of the pledge is to detain it as security until the debt is paid, not to force a sale. The creditor may keep the entire pledge even after partial payment until the full debt is recovered, similar to the detention of a sold item.
2. When the debtor pays the full debt, the creditor must surrender the pledge. However, the pledge is not legally dissolved until actual return; it remains in the creditor's liability (مضمون) until surrendered or the debt is formally discharged. If the pledge perishes after payment but before delivery, the debtor recovers his payment because the destruction reveals satisfaction from the original possession. If both parties cancel the pledge contract, it does not dissolve while still in the creditor's hand; the creditor may still recover his debt. If the pledge perishes after cancellation, it is lost against the debt, but if it perishes after formal discharge (إبراء), the creditor is not liable (by istihsan) because the pledge no longer exists (required both possession and debt).
3. The creditor is prohibited from using the pledge (e.g., employing, residing, wearing, leasing, lending). The pledge is only for detention; any beneficial use is forbidden unless the debtor permits it. This preserves the trust nature of the possession.

Key Terms
الحبس الدائم (al-habs al-da'im): Permanent detention of the pledge as security until the debt is paid.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Formal discharge of the debt by the creditor without receiving payment.
الاستيفاء بعد الاستيفاء (al-istifa' ba'da al-istifa'): Double satisfaction: when payment is made after the creditor has already been deemed satisfied (e.g., by destruction).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the pledge perishes after the debtor pays the full debt but before the creditor returns it to him?
A) The debtor loses his payment.
B) The debtor can recover his payment because the destruction shows the creditor was already satisfied.
C) The creditor is obligated to pay the value of the pledge.
D) The pledge is considered lost against the debt.

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee's possession of a pledge is strictly for security: he cannot be forced to sell, must surrender upon payment, and cannot use the pledge without permission.

Pledgee's Use of Collateral: Permission and Liability
Ruling on pledgee's use of collateral with and without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a pledgee's use of collateral and distinguish the legal consequences with and without the pledgor's permission.

Core Idea
Without permission, use is usurpation; with permission, it becomes a loan (ariyah) and accidental loss does not affect the debt.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, expanding on al-Mabsut, clarifies a critical aspect of pledge (rahn): whether the pledgee may benefit from the collateral. The ruling turns on the pledgor's permission and fundamentally changes the nature of possession and liability. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the contract of rahn in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is the pledgee's use of the collateral. The default ruling, as stated, is prohibition: the pledgee may not benefit without permission.
The text explicitly says 'ليس للمرتهن أن ينتفع' – it is not permissible.
2
Locate the authority cited
The passage cites al-Mabsut by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, a foundational Zahir al-Riwayah text. This establishes the ruling as part of the authentic Hanafi tradition.
Al-Mabsut is one of the six core books of the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the two scenarios
Scenario 1: Permission granted – the pledgee may use the collateral as permitted, and possession becomes that of a loan (ariyah). Scenario 2: No permission – the pledgee becomes a guarantor and a possessor by usurpation (ghasb).
The change in possession type is the key legal mechanism.
4
Analyze the effect on the debt if collateral perishes
With permission: if the collateral perishes during use, nothing of the debt is dropped because the collateral was held as ariyah. Without permission: the pledgee is liable for the value as a usurper, but the debt remains due.
The debt is independent of the pledge's safety in the permitted scenario.
5
Note the consequence of transgression on the pledge
The text emphasizes that transgression (using without permission) does not invalidate the pledge itself. The pledge remains valid, but the pledgee incurs liability.
The contract of rahn is not voided by misuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكمه وهو الضمان إلى فعل كان متعدياً ولا يبطل الرهن بالتعدي. قال في المبسوط : وليس للمرتهن أن ينتفع بالمرهون إلا بإذن الراهن فإذا أذن له جاز أن يفعل ما إذن له فيه ولو فعل من غير إذن صار ضامناً بحكم الرهن بحكم وقابضاً الغصب. وإن ترك الاستعمال عاد لكونه رهناً، ولو استعمل الرهن بإذن المرتهن[؟] فإن هلك حالة الانتفاع لم يسقط من الدين شيء لأنه بالإذن صار مقبوضاً بحكم العارية، وإن خالف وهلك في حال الاستعمال يضمن ضمان الغصب.
Its ruling is that it is a guarantee. And he is not a guarantor until he commits an act that is transgressive, but the pledge is not invalidated by transgression. He said in al-Mabsut: The pledgee may not benefit from the collateral except with the permission of the pledgor. If the pledgor permits him, he may do what he permitted. If he does it without permission, he becomes a guarantor by the ruling of the pledge and a possessor by usurpation. If he abandons use, it returns to being a pledge. If he uses the pledge with permission of the pledgee[?] and it perishes during use, nothing of the debt is dropped because, by permission, it becomes possessed under the ruling of a loan (ariyah). But if he violates [the terms] and it perishes during use, he guarantees it as a usurper.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the pledgee's use of the collateral. It begins by stating the general rule that the pledgee cannot benefit from the collateral without the pledgor's permission. If permission is granted, the use is valid, and if the collateral perishes during such use, the debt remains intact because the collateral is now held as a loan (ariyah). However, if the pledgee uses it without permission, he becomes a guarantor and liable as a usurper. The text also notes that transgression does not void the pledge. The citation from al-Mabsut (a foundational Hanafi text) reinforces these points.

Key Terms
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The person who receives the pledge as security for a debt (the pledgee).
الراهن (al-rahin): The person who gives the pledge as security (the pledgor).
عارية (ariyah): A loan of an object for use: transfer of possession without transfer of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the pledgor permits the pledgee to use the collateral, and the collateral perishes during use, what happens to the debt?
A) The debt is reduced by the value of the collateral.
B) The debt is not reduced because the collateral was held as a loan.
C) The pledgee is liable for the value and must repay it.
D) The pledge is voided and the pledgor loses his right.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the pledgee's use of collateral is prohibited without permission; with permission, it becomes a loan (ariyah) and accidental loss does not reduce the debt.

Burden of Proof in Collateral Damage: The Maxim 'Al-Qawl Qawl'
Ruling on burden of proof for damage to collateral

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of evidence (al-qawl qawl) to determine who bears the burden of proof when collateral is damaged during authorized use.

Core Idea
When collateral is damaged, the pledgor's word prevails if he denies use; but if use is admitted, the pledgee's word prevails.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced ruling from al-Muntaqa about conflicting claims over torn collateral. The key is the application of the maxim 'al-qawl qawl' (the word is the word of...) which shifts depending on whether the pledgor admits the pledgee's use. Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly assigning liability in collateral disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct factual situations: (1) pledgor denies the pledgee wore the garment at all; (2) pledgor admits the pledgee wore it but disputes when the tear occurred.
The maxim applies differently in each.
2
Apply 'al-qawl qawl' to scenario 1
When the pledgor denies use, the pledgee is claiming exemption from liability (by saying the tear happened during authorized use). The pledgor denies this right, so his statement is taken (he is the defendant). The pledgee bears no burden of proof.
The pledgee's claim is an assertion of a right (exemption), so he must prove it; here he cannot.
3
Apply 'al-qawl qawl' to scenario 2
When the pledgor admits use, the pledgee's claim that the tear occurred from his wearing becomes plausible (wearing is an apparent cause of tearing). Now the pledgor must prove the tear pre-existed; otherwise, the pledgee's word prevails. The burden shifts to the pledgor.
The maxim 'al-qawl qawl' now favors the pledgee because of the apparent cause.
4
Identify the underlying principle
The rule is that where a claim is supported by an apparent cause (zahir), the burden of proof shifts to the opponent. When no apparent cause exists, the default rule favoring the defendant applies.
This is a specific application of the broader Hanafi principle about apparent evidence (al-zahir yushhad lil-mudda'i).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى : وإذا كان الرهن ثوباً فأذن له الراهن في لبسه يوماً ثم جاء به متخرقاً فقال المرتهن تخرق من لبسه من ذلك اليوم فقال الراهن لم يتخرق من لبسك ولم تلبسه فالقول قول الراهن لأن المرتهن ادعى البراءة عن الضمان لاستعمال الثوب بإذن الراهن وهو ينكر فيكون القول له، فإذا أقر الراهن أنه لبسه في ذلك اليوم وتخرق قبل لبسه أو بعده فالقول قول المرتهن أنه تخرق من لبسه والبينة بينة الراهن لأن الظاهر شاهد للمرتهن لأن فعله وهو اللبس سبب التخرق ظاهراً وغير موهوم فيه في حال التخرق في السبب الظاهر دون الموهوم والله تعالى أعلم.
And in al-Muntaqa: If the collateral is a garment and the pledgor allows the pledgee to wear it for a day, then he brings it torn, and the pledgee says, 'It became torn from my wearing it that day,' and the pledgor says, 'It did not become torn from your wearing it, and you did not wear it,' then the word is the pledgor's because the pledgee claims exemption from liability for using the garment with the pledgor's permission, and the pledgor denies it, so the word is his. If the pledgor admits that he wore it that day but it became torn before or after his wearing, then the word is the pledgee's that it became torn from his wearing, and the burden of proof is on the pledgor because the apparent evidence supports the pledgee: his act of wearing is an apparent cause of tearing, not speculative, and in cases where the cause is apparent, [the ruling favors the one with apparent evidence] over the speculative. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage from al-Muntaqa addresses a dispute over damage to a pledged garment. If the pledgee claims the damage occurred during his authorized use, but the pledgor denies that the pledgee used it at all, the pledgor's statement is believed because the pledgee is claiming exemption from liability. However, if the pledgor admits the use but disputes when the tear occurred, the pledgee's claim is believed because use is an apparent cause of damage. In this latter case, the burden of proof rests with the pledgor if he claims the tear existed before use.

Key Terms
القول قول (al-qawl qawl): The legal maxim that in conflicting claims, one party's statement is given precedence; here the pledgor when denying use, the pledgee when use is admitted.
الظاهر (al-zahir): Apparent evidence or outward circumstance that makes a claim plausible, such as wearing being a common cause of tearing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the pledgor admits the pledgee used the garment but disputes when the tear occurred, what is the ruling regarding the burden of proof?
A) The pledgee must prove the tear occurred during his use.
B) The pledgor must prove the tear existed before use.
C) Both must bring evidence, and a judge decides.
D) The pledgee's word alone is insufficient; an expert must assess.

Key Takeaway
The burden of proof in collateral disputes shifts based on whether the pledgor admits the pledgee's use, because admitted use provides an apparent cause for damage.

Pledgee's Travel and Loss of Pledged Property
Rulings on pledgee's travel and loss of pledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the pledgee's travel with the pledge and determine the effect on the debt when the pledged property is destroyed without the pledgee's fault.

Core Idea
The pledgee may travel with the pledge; destruction without fault does not reduce the debt.

This lesson covers two scenarios from the text: the permissibility of the pledgee traveling with the pledged item, and how damages (like burning of date palms or killing of a pledged slave) affect the debt. The key principle is that the debt remains unchanged unless the pledgee is negligent. A difference of opinion arises between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the case of a sick slave killed.

Guided Path
1
Travel of the pledgee
Identify the ruling from al-Ghiyathiyyah: the pledgee may travel with the pledge regardless of transport costs.
Muhammad compares this to a deposit – the pledgee is like a trustee.
2
Slave killed without fault
Consider the case where a pledged sick slave is killed (e.g., by a third party). According to Abu Hanifa, the debt remains unchanged. This is contrary to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad who hold that the debt is reduced.
The difference hinges on whether the pledgee's right is attached to the specific object or its value.
3
Burning of palm trees
If the pledged palm trees burn, the loss is proportionally subtracted from the debt.
This is consistent with the principle that partial loss reduces the security proportionately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يتعرض المؤلف للسعر بالعين المزهونة ولا لما إذا أعير الرهن للمرتهن قال الغيائية: وللمرتهن أن يسافر بالرهن إذا كان له حمل ومؤنة أو لم يكن. وعن محمد أنه كالوديعة رهن المرتهن وارتهانه موقوف ولو رهن عبداً مريضاً فقتل فالدين على حاله خلافاً لهما، وكذا إذا قتل قصاصاً بعمد أو بسرقة ويصدق المرتهن أنه كان هكذا، ولو احترق النخل ذهب بحصته. وفي الخانية : رهن عبداً وغاب ثم إن المرتهن وجد العبد حراً، فإن كان العبد أقر بالرق عند الرهن لم يرجع المرتهن بدينه عليه. أخذت المرأة بصداقها المسمى رهناً يساوي صداقها ثم وهبت صداقها من الزوج أو أبرأته كان عليها رد الرهن إلى الزوج، فإن هلك الرهن عندها يهلك بغير شيء، ولو اختلعت المرأة من زوجها بعدما وهبت مهرها كان عليها رد الرهن ولا يبطل الرهن بموت الراهن ولا بموت المرتهن ولا بموتهما وبقي الرهن رهناً عند الورثة وسيأتي له مزيد بيان.
The author did not address the price of the pledged object nor when the pledge is loaned to the pledgee. Al-Ghiyathiyyah said: The pledgee may travel with the pledge whether it has freight and expense or not. From Muhammad: It is like a deposit; the pledgee's pledging and receiving pledges is suspended. If he pledges a sick slave and it is killed, the debt remains as is, contrary to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?]. Similarly, if killed by retribution intentionally or by theft, and the pledgee is believed that it was like that. If the palm trees burn, their share goes. In al-Khaniyyah: He pledged a slave and disappeared, then the pledgee found the slave free. If the slave admitted to being a slave at the time of pledge, the pledgee does not recover his debt from him. A woman took as her dower a pledge equal to her dower, then she gifted her dower to her husband or released him from it; she must return the pledge to the husband. If the pledge perishes with her, it perishes for nothing. If the woman divorces by khul' after gifting her dower, she must return the pledge. The pledge is not invalidated by the death of the pledgor nor the death of the pledgee nor their deaths; the pledge remains a pledge with the heirs, and further explanation will come.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage presents a series of isolated rulings on pledge. It begins by noting that the author did not discuss the price of the pledged object or loaning the pledge to the pledgee. Then it cites al-Ghiyathiyyah allowing the pledgee to travel with the pledge. From Muhammad (al-Shaybani), it compares the pledge to a deposit. Several cases are given: a sick slave killed (debt unchanged), burning of palm trees (proportionate reduction), a slave found free (if he confessed to slavery, no recourse against him), and a woman who pledges her dower then forgives it (must return pledge). The key rule stated at the end: death of parties does not terminate the pledge; it passes to heirs.

Key Terms
صداق (sadaq): The dower (mahr) given by the husband to the wife at marriage.
خلع (khul'): Divorce at the wife's request in exchange for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if a pledged slave is killed without the pledgee's fault, what happens to the debt?
A) The debt is reduced by the slave's value.
B) The debt remains unchanged.
C) The debt is canceled.
D) The pledgee must compensate the pledgor.

Key Takeaway
The debt secured by a pledge remains unaffected by accidental loss of the pledged property, as long as the pledgee is not negligent.

Pledge and Dower: Forgiveness and Khul‘
Ruling on pledge taken as dower and subsequent forgiveness or khul‘.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling that when a woman takes a pledge as her dower and then forgives the dower, she must return the pledge; and the same applies in case of khul‘.

Core Idea
Forgiving the dower cancels the right to hold the pledge.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from the text: a woman receives a pledge equal to her dower, then either forgives the dower (hiba or ibra) or divorces by khul‘. The pledge must be returned, and if it perishes in her possession, she is not liable to compensate.

Guided Path
1
Woman takes pledge as dower
The woman accepts a pledge (e.g., a piece of jewelry) in lieu of her dower. The pledge is equal in value to her mahr.
The pledge serves as security for the dower.
2
She forgives the dower
She either gifts the dower to her husband (hiba) or releases him from it (ibra). The basis for holding the pledge disappears.
Forgiveness of the debt cancels the security.
3
Ruling on the pledge
She must return the pledge to the husband. If it perishes in her hands, she is not liable because she was holding it as a trust until the dower was paid.
Once the dower is forgiven, her possession becomes wrongful, but destruction before knowledge or return is excused.
4
Khul‘ case
If she divorces by khul‘ after forgiving the dower, she must still return the pledge. The khul‘ does not change the obligation.
Khul‘ is a separate transaction; the pledge remains due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
A wife took a pledge as her dower, then she forgave the dower. The pledge perished in her possession. What is the ruling?
A) She must compensate the husband for the pledge.
B) She owes nothing.
C) She must return an equivalent pledge.
D) The husband can deduct the value from any future gifts.

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness of the dower cancels the right to hold the pledge, but the pledgee is not liable for accidental loss.

Continuity of Pledge After Death and the Free Slave Case
Rulings on continuity of pledge after death and the free slave case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain that the pledge remains valid after the death of the pledgor or pledgee, and determine the outcome when a pledged slave is found to be free.

Core Idea
Death does not dissolve the pledge; a slave's confession of slavery at pledge time bars recourse.

This lesson covers two important rules: first, the death of either the pledgor or pledgee does not invalidate the pledge; it continues with the heirs. Second, if a pledged slave is later discovered to have always been free, the outcome depends on whether the slave admitted to being a slave at the time of the pledge.

Guided Path
1
Death of parties does not invalidate pledge
The text explicitly states that the pledge remains valid after the death of the pledgor, the pledgee, or both. It passes to the heirs as a security.
The pledge is an obligation that survives death.
2
Free slave case: slave confessed to slavery
If a man pledges a slave, then the slave goes missing, and later the pledgee finds the slave free. The question is whether the pledgee can recover the debt from the slave.
The slave's status at the time of pledge is key.
3
If the slave admitted to being a slave at the time of pledge
If the slave himself admitted that he was a slave when the pledge was made, then the pledgee cannot recover the debt from the slave (i.e., the slave is not liable). The pledgee's recourse is against the pledgor.
The admission binds the slave; he cannot later claim freedom to avoid the pledge.
4
Implication
This prevents the slave from using his freedom to escape liability; but also protects the free person if he was truly free and never admitted.
The text does not specify the ruling if the slave never admitted; presumably the pledgee could not enforce against a free person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يتعرض المؤلف للسعر بالعين المزهونة ولا لما إذا أعير الرهن للمرتهن قال الغيائية: وللمرتهن أن يسافر بالرهن إذا كان له حمل ومؤنة أو لم يكن. وعن محمد أنه كالوديعة رهن المرتهن وارتهانه موقوف ولو رهن عبداً مريضاً فقتل فالدين على حاله خلافاً لهما، وكذا إذا قتل قصاصاً بعمد أو بسرقة ويصدق المرتهن أنه كان هكذا، ولو احترق النخل ذهب بحصته. وفي الخانية : رهن عبداً وغاب ثم إن المرتهن وجد العبد حراً، فإن كان العبد أقر بالرق عند الرهن لم يرجع المرتهن بدينه عليه. أخذت المرأة بصداقها المسمى رهناً يساوي صداقها ثم وهبت صداقها من الزوج أو أبرأته كان عليها رد الرهن إلى الزوج، فإن هلك الرهن عندها يهلك بغير شيء، ولو اختلعت المرأة من زوجها بعدما وهبت مهرها كان عليها رد الرهن ولا يبطل الرهن بموت الراهن ولا بموت المرتهن ولا بموتهما وبقي الرهن رهناً عند الورثة وسيأتي له مزيد بيان.

Which of the following is true regarding the pledge upon the death of the pledgor?
A) The pledge is automatically void.
B) The pledge remains valid with the heirs.

D) The pledge is transferred to the state.

Key Takeaway
The pledge is a durable right that survives the death of both parties; it is not automatically extinguished.
